The Sixth Proof: Motive Destroys Free Choice
The author presents the sixth proof that dependence on motive destroys free choice.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical argument that if actions depend on motives, then free choice is impossible, and why this argument is considered a refutation of determinism.

Core Idea
Motive-based action destroys the difference between necessary and free agents.

This passage presents the sixth proof against the view that actions require motives. The author argues that if actions are tied to motives, then actions become as necessary as natural phenomena, erasing human choice. This contradicts our direct knowledge that we act freely.

Guided Path
1
Read the Source Passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice how the author presents the argument in two parts: first showing that motive dependence leads to necessity, then showing that this contradicts necessary knowledge.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Understand the Two States
Identify the two states: when the motive is present, the act is necessary; when absent, impossible. There is no third state. This means the agent has no genuine ability or choice in any situation.
This is the core of the proof: it reduces the free agent to a necessary agent.
3
Connect to Necessary Knowledge
The author then appeals to necessary sciences: we intuitively know the difference between a human acting by choice and a stone falling by nature. If motive dependence were true, this distinction would vanish, which is absurd.
This shows the proof is a reductio ad absurdum.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة السادسة : إن توقف الفعل على الداعي يوجب أن لا يبقى فرق بين الموجب بالذات وبين الفاعل المختار . وهذا باطل . فذا باطل . بيان الأول : إنا سنبين أن عند حصول الداعية يجب الفعل وجوباً ضرورياً ، لا يمكن نقضه ، وعند عدم الداعية يمتنع وقوع الفعل ، ومعلوم أنه لا حال غير هاتين الحالتين ، فإذا كان الفعل في إحدى الحالتين واجباً ، وفي الحالة الثانية ممتنعاً. فحينئذ يلزم أن لا يحصل التمكن والاختيار في شيء من الأحوال البتة . فيثبت أن توقف الفعل على حصول الداعي، يقتضي أن لا يبقى فرق البتة بين الموجب بالذات وبين الفاعل المختار . وبيان أن هذا باطل : إنه من جملة العلوم الضرورية : الفرق بين كون الإنسان متحركاً بقدرته واختياره وبين كون الحجر نازلاً بطبعه ، وكون النار محرقة بطبعها ، فيثبت : أن القول بتوقف الفعل على الداعي : يفضي إلى الباطل المذكور ، فوجب أن يكون باطلاً
The sixth proof: That the dependence of the act upon the motive necessitates that there remain no difference between the necessary-by-essence and the free agent. And this is false. So that is false. Explanation of the first: We will show that when the motive is present, the act is necessitated with a necessary necessity that cannot be broken, and when the motive is absent, the occurrence of the act is impossible, and it is known that there is no state other than these two states. So if the act is necessary in one of the two states and impossible in the second state, then it follows that ability and choice do not occur in any state at all. So it is established that the dependence of the act upon the presence of the motive necessitates that no difference remains at all between the necessary-by-essence and the free agent. And the explanation that this is false: That it is from the necessary sciences: the difference between a human moving by his power and choice and a stone falling by its nature, and a fire burning by its nature. So it is established that the statement that the act depends on the motive leads to the mentioned falsehood, so it must be false.
Source pages p. 41

Commentary
This passage is the sixth proof against the dependence of action on motive. The author argues that if action depends on motive, then there is no difference between the necessary agent (like heat causing fire) and the free agent (like a human choosing). Because when the motive is present, the action is necessary; when absent, impossible. Thus choice and ability are eliminated. This contradicts the necessary knowledge that humans act by their power and choice, unlike natural phenomena like a stone falling or fire burning. Therefore, the claim that action depends on motive is false.

Key Terms
الموجب بالذات
الفاعل المختار
الداعية
العلوم الضرورية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that motives provide a rational basis for choice, enhancing freedom.
Correction: The proof shows that if motives determine actions, then actions are necessitated, eliminating genuine choice. Motives would make the agent like a natural cause.

Checkpoint
The sixth proof argues that if action depends on motive, then what necessary distinction is lost?
A) Between essence and attributes.
B) Between the necessary-by-essence and the free agent.
C) Between existence and non-existence.
D) Between knowledge and ignorance.

Key Takeaway
The sixth proof shows that if actions were dependent on motives, the distinction between necessary agents (like fire) and free agents (humans) would collapse, contradicting our self-evident knowledge of free choice.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at your own daily choices, do you ever feel that you are compelled by your desires or circumstances? In light of this lesson, how would you differentiate between being genuinely free and simply being driven by a motive you 'couldn't resist'? What is one choice you make regularly that you now realize you must take full responsibility for, without excusing it as inevitable?
?
The author says the difference between a human acting by choice and a stone falling is self-evident. Is your own experience of free choice really that clear? Have there been moments when you felt as though you had no choice, as if you were just acting out of nature? What does this lesson teach you about the danger of shrinking your sense of moral responsibility by claiming 'I had no option'?
?
This proof shows that if an act depends on a motive, the agent is no different from a necessary agent like fire. In your spiritual life, do you tend to wait for a 'motive' to worship or obey Allah—such as a strong feeling or conducive circumstances—rather than acting out of sheer will and duty? How can you break free from this dependency and exercise true choice in your obedience to Allah starting today?

Responses to the First and Second Proofs
Opponents answer the first proof and begin answering the second.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the opponents answer the first proof by redefining the term 'capable' and using an analogy from contingency, and how they begin to answer the second proof.

Core Idea
The opponents reframe 'capable' to require motive, not mere power.

This passage records the opponents' rejoinder to the author's proofs. They challenge the very definition of 'capable' to undermine the first proof, then strengthen their position with an analogy from contingency. The second answer is incomplete but starts by addressing the engrossed thinker. Pay close attention to how they use language to shift the burden of proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its literal translation without stopping for analysis. Let the opponents' argument sink in as a whole.
Notice the formal structure: they begin each answer with 'أما الجواب عن...'.
2
Identify the two-part answer to the first proof
The first proof is answered in two parts: a definitional question about what 'capable' means, and then a strengthening argument from contingency. Find where each part begins.
The definitional part asks 'ما المراد من قولكم...' and the contingency part begins with 'وبما يقوي هذا الكلام...'.
3
Analyze the redefinition strategy
The opponents argue that if 'capable' means 'can act without motive', it's the point in dispute; if it means 'can act with motive', it's conceded but doesn't prove mere capability suffices. This is a classic dialectical move.
This shows the opponents are not denying the proof outright but reframing the terms.
4
Note the incomplete second answer
The answer to the second proof is cut off mid-sentence. It begins to address the scenario of an engrossed thinker, but the text ends. Reflect on why this might be significant.
The lesson title includes both answers, but we only have a fragment of the second.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما القائلون بأن الفعل يتوقف على الداعي . فقد أجابوا عن هذه الدلائل بأجوبة واضحة قوية . وقالوا : أما الجواب عن الحجة الأولى : هو أن يقال : ما المراد من قولكم : إن القادر يجب أن يصح صدور الفعل منه ؟ إن أردتم به : أن القادر هو الذي يصح صدور الفعل عنه بمجرد كونه قادراً ، فهذا عين ما وقع النزاع فيه. فإن عندنا مجرد كونه قادراً لا يكفي في صحة صدور الفعل عنه ، بل ما لم ينضم إليه الداعي ، فإنه يمتنع صدور الفعل فقولكم : بأن مجرد كونه قادراً ، يكفي في صحة صدور الفعل عنه عين محل النزاع. وإن أردتم به : أن القادر هو الذي لا يصح صدور الفعل منه عند انضمام الداعي إليه ، فهذا مسلم ، إلا أن هذا القدر لا يدل على أن مجرد كونه قادراً يكفي في صحة صدور الفعل عنه ، فيثبت أن هذا الكلام مغالطة وبما يقوي هذا الكلام : أنا نقول : لا شك أن الجائز هو الذي يصح بدلاً عن العدم ويصح عدمه بدلاً عن الوجود ، ولما استوى وجب الافتقار إلى المخصص فيقال : لما سلمتم أن الجائز يصح عليه الطرفان . فإنه نظراً إلى ذاته ، وعند فرض تلك الماهية خالية عن المؤثر ، وجب أن يصح رجحان وجوده على عدمه ، إذ لو لم يصح هذا الرجحان لقدح ذلك في قولنا : إنه من حيث هو هو يصح عليه كل واحد من الطرفين، ولما ثبت صحة حصول هذا الرجحان ، حال عدم المؤثر المنفصل، علمنا : أن حصول هذا الرجحان ، لا يتوقف على وجود المؤثر المنفصل، وكما أن هذا الكلام باطل ، فكذلك الكلام الذي ذكرتموه يجب أن يكون باطلاً . وأما الجواب من الحجة الثانية : وهي قولهم : إن من يكون مستغرق الفكر في مهم من المهمات
And as for those who say that the act depends on the motive, they have answered these proofs with clear and strong answers, and they said: As for the answer to the first proof: It is to say: What do you mean by your statement: The capable one must have the act proceed from him? If you mean by it that the capable one is the one from whom the act can proceed simply by being capable, then this is exactly what is disputed. For with us, merely being capable is not sufficient for the act to proceed from him; rather, as long as the motive is not conjoined, the act cannot proceed from him. So your statement that merely being capable is sufficient for the act to proceed from him is exactly the point of dispute. And if you mean by it that the capable one is the one from whom the act does not proceed when the motive is conjoined, then this is granted, but this amount does not indicate that merely being capable is sufficient for the act to proceed from him. So it is established that this speech is a fallacy. And what strengthens this speech: We say: There is no doubt that the contingent is that which can exist instead of non-existence and can non-exist instead of existence, and since the two sides are equal, need for a specifier is necessitated. So it is said: Since you have conceded that the contingent can have both sides, then with respect to its essence and upon the supposition of that quiddity being devoid of any effect, its existence must be preferable to its non-existence, for if this preferability did not occur, that would detract from our statement that it, in itself, can have each of the two sides. And when the existence of this preferability is established without the separate effect, we know that the occurrence of this preferability does not depend on the existence of the separate effect. And just as this speech is false, so too the speech that you have mentioned must be false. And as for the answer to the second proof: Which is their statement: That the one whose thought is engrossed in an important matter...
Source pages p. 41

Commentary
This passage records the opponents' answers to the first two proofs. The opponents reframe the definition of capability: they ask what is meant by 'the capable one must have the act proceed from him.' If it means mere capability suffices, that is the point of contention; if it means capability plus motive suffices, then it is granted but does not prove that mere capability is enough. Then they strengthen this by analogy with contingency: a contingent thing requires a specifier; even without an external effect, it can have a preference for existence over non-existence. They argue that similarly, the agent's ability to act does not depend on a separate motive. The answer to the second proof is cut off, but it begins by addressing the case of an engrossed thinker.

Key Terms
القادر
الداعي
الجائز
المخصص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that redefining capability solves the issue, but the opponent's response may not address the core argument.
Correction: While the redefinition exposes a potential ambiguity, it does not resolve whether action actually requires a motive. The opponents still need to prove that motive is necessary, not just redefine terms.

Checkpoint
In the answer to the first proof, what is the opponents' main strategy?
A) They deny that action requires a motive.
B) They redefine the term 'capable' to include the presence of motive.
C) They argue that slow motion is irrelevant.
D) They claim that the agent is always aware.

Key Takeaway
The opponents answer the first proof by redefining 'capable' to require motive, and they strengthen this by an analogy with contingency, but the second answer is left incomplete.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The opponents argue that mere capability is insufficient for action; a motive must be present. Examine the motives behind your daily acts of worship: are they driven by genuine desire for Allah's pleasure, or have they become empty habits? How can you transform your practice to be more intentional starting today?
?
This passage shows how a disagreement often boils down to definitions. In your relationships, when you disagree with someone, do you first clarify what each of you means by key terms? Commit to doing this in your next disagreement to avoid unnecessary conflict.

Answer to the First Argument: The Meaning of Capability
Answer to the first argument: disambiguating 'capability' and analogizing to the possible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the claim that mere capability suffices for action, and why a motive is necessary.

Core Idea
Capability alone does not produce action; a motive must be present.

The opponent argues that a capable agent must be able to act simply by being capable. The author challenges this by clarifying what is meant by 'capable.' He exposes the ambiguity and shows that the opponent's claim is either the very point in dispute or a conceded triviality. He then reinforces his position using the analogy of the possible thing requiring a specifier.

Guided Path
1
Understand the opponent's claim
The opponent claims that a capable agent must have the possibility of action proceeding from him. Read the first part of the passage to see how the author sets up the analysis.
Notice the phrase 'ما المراد من قولكم' – the author is asking for clarification.
2
Follow the author's analysis of two meanings
The author distinguishes two interpretations of the opponent's claim. One is that mere capability suffices for action; the other is that capability plus a motive suffices. See how each is treated.
The first is exactly the dispute; the second is conceded but does not prove the opponent's point.
3
Grasp the analogy with the possible
The author introduces the concept of al-ja'iz (the possible) which is neutral between existence and non-existence and therefore needs a specifier. Understand the analogy between capability and the possible.
Just as the possible cannot preponderate without an external influence, capability cannot produce action without a motive.
4
See how the analogy proves the author's point
The author argues that if the opponent's reasoning were correct, it would imply that the possible can preponderate itself, which is false. Therefore, the opponent's argument is false too.
This is a reductio ad absurdum: the opponent's view leads to an absurd consequence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما الجواب عن الحجة الأولى : هو أن يقال : ما المراد من قولكم : إن القادر يجب أن يصح صدور الفعل منه ؟ عنه إن أردتم به : أن القادر هو الذي يصح صدور الفعل عنه بمجرد كونه قادراً ، فهذا عين ما وقع النزاع فيه. فإن عندنا مجرد كونه قادراً لا يكفي في صحة صدور الفعل عنه ، بل ما لم ينضم إليه الداعي ، فإنه يمتنع صدور الفعل فقولكم : بأن مجرد كونه قادراً ، يكفي في صحة صدور الفعل عنه عين محل النزاع. وإن أردتم به : أن القادر هو الذي لا يصح (۱) صدور الفعل منه عند انضمام الداعي إليه ، فهذا مسلم ، إلا أن هذا القدر لا يدل على أن مجرد كونه قادراً يكفي في صحة صدور الفعل عنه ، فيثبت أن هذا الكلام مغالطة وبما يقوي هذا الكلام : أنا نقول : لا شك أن الجائز هو الذي يصح بدلاً عن العدم ويصح عدمه بدلاً عن الوجود ، ولما استوى وجب الافتقار إلى المخصص فيقال : لما سلمتم أن الجائز يصح عليه الطرفان . فإنه نظراً إلى ذاته ، وعند فرض تلك الماهية خالية عن المؤثر ، وجب أن يصح رجحان وجوده على عدمه ، إذ لو لم يصح هذا الرجحان لقدح ذلك في قولنا : إنه من حيث هو هـو يصح عليه كل واحد من الطرفين، ولما ثبت صحة (٢) حصول هذا الرجحان ، حال عدم المؤثر المنفصل، علمنا : أن حصول هذا الرجحان ، لا يتوقف على وجود المؤثر المنفصل، وكما أن هذا الكلام باطل ، فكذلك الكلام ) الذي ذكرتموه يجب أن يكون باطلاً .
As for the answer to the first argument: it is to say: what is intended by your statement that 'the capable one must have the possibility of action proceeding from him'? If you intend by it that the capable one is he from whom the action can proceed merely by virtue of his being capable, then this is exactly the point of dispute. For with us, mere capability is not sufficient for the possibility of action proceeding from him; rather, as long as the motive (al-dā‘ī) is not joined to him, the action is impossible. So your statement that mere capability is sufficient for the possibility of action is exactly the point of contention. And if you intend by it that the capable one is he from whom action does not proceed (1) when the motive is joined to him, then this is conceded, but this much does not indicate that mere capability suffices for the possibility of action. Thus it is established that this statement is a fallacy. And what strengthens this statement: we say: there is no doubt that the possible (al-jā’iz) is that for which existence is possible instead of non-existence and non-existence is possible instead of existence. When it is equal, the need for a specifier (al-mukhassis) becomes necessary. So it is said: since you conceded that the possible admits both sides, then with respect to its essence, and upon assuming that quiddity devoid of any influence, it must be that the preponderance of its existence over its non-existence is possible; for if this preponderance were not possible, that would impugn our statement that 'it is such that each of the two sides is possible for it.' And when the possibility of this preponderance is established in the absence of an external influence, we know that the occurrence of this preponderance does not depend on the existence of an external influence. And just as this statement is false, so too the statement you mentioned must be false.
Source pages p. 42

Commentary
This passage is an answer to the first argument, which likely claimed that a capable agent must be able to act simply because he is capable. The author dissects the opponent's statement by asking what they mean by 'capability'. If they mean that capability alone suffices for action, then that is precisely the point in dispute, because according to the author, without a motive (al-dā‘ī) the act is impossible. If they mean something else—that the capable one is the one who can act when a motive is present—then that is conceded, but it does not prove that mere capability suffices. Thus the opponent's argument is a fallacy. The author then reinforces this by discussing the possible (al-jā’iz): a possible thing, considered in itself, equally admits existence and non-existence, so it needs a specifier (al-mukhassis) to tip the balance. The opponent's claim that mere capability (like a possible thing's essence) suffices for action is analogous to saying that a possible thing, without an external influence, can preponderate itself—which is false. Therefore, the opponent's argument is also false.

Key Terms
القادر (al-qādir, the capable)
الداعي (al-dā‘ī, the motive)
الجائز (al-jā’iz, the possible)
المخصص (al-mukhassis, the specifier)
المؤثر (al-mu’aththir, the influence)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if someone has the ability to do something, then they can do it without any further condition.
Correction: The passage shows that capability alone is insufficient; a motive (al-dā‘ī) is also required. Without a motive, the action is impossible.

Checkpoint
What does the author identify as the real issue in the first argument?
A) Whether the capable one can act without a motive
B) Whether the capable one is defined by having the ability to act
C) Whether the possible thing needs a specifier
D) Whether the opponent's argument is a fallacy

Key Takeaway
The capability of an agent does not guarantee action; a motive is required, just as a possible thing requires a specifier to exist.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you find yourself capable of doing a good deed but procrastinate, do you recognize that your capability alone is insufficient and that you lack the sincere motive? What specific motive do you need to seek from Allah to turn your capability into action?
?
In what area of your life have you been assuming that your ability is enough, only to find that you fail to act? How does this lesson challenge you to seek the right motives, not just rely on your power?
?
The possible thing requires an external specifier to exist. Consider your own existence – do you truly live with the awareness that every moment of your life is sustained by the Specifier, Allah? How does that awareness change your attitude toward your daily actions and intentions?

The Motive Behind Unconscious Movements
Answer to the second argument: unconscious finger movement during deep thought is still motivated.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how seemingly unconscious actions still have a motive, and the three ways a motive can be present even when not apparent.

Core Idea
Every action has a motive, even if it is subtle or fleeting.

This passage tackles the objection that some actions occur without a motive, like moving your finger while lost in thought. The author argues that such actions are still motivated, and he gives three specific ways a motive can be present. By examining this argument, you will see the depth of the principle that every voluntary act requires a motive.

Guided Path
1
Understand the opponent's claim
Read the passage and identify the argument that a person deeply absorbed in thought can move a finger without a motive. This is the challenge to the principle that all voluntary acts require a motive.
The opponent uses an example of unconscious movement.
2
Follow the author's initial response
The author replies that the finger moves only if the agent intends it. So even if the agent is unaware of the intention, it is present.
This upholds the principle.
3
Examine the opponent's counter
The opponent then says that even if intention exists, there is no apparent benefit. The author denies this and asserts there is some pleasure or benefit.
4
Learn the three reasons
The author provides three possibilities: boredom relief, habit, and fleeting imagination. Each shows a plausible motive.
These are not exhaustive but sufficient to refute the opponent.
5
Reflect on the lesson
Consider how even habitual or automatic actions have motives, often subtle. This deepens your understanding of motivation in voluntary acts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الجواب (1) من الحجة الثانية : وهي قولهم : إن من يكون مستغرق الفكر في مهم من المهمات لفقد يحصل تحرك إصبعه في تلك الحالة ، مع أنه يكون غافلاً عن تلك الحركة . فقد حصل الفعل من غير الداعي. فالجواب عنه : إنه ما لم يقصد ذلك الفاعل إلى تحريك الإصبع ، فإنه لا يتحرك . فإن قالوا : فهب أن الأمر كذلك ، إلا أنه ليس له في ذلك الفعل منفعة أصلا ، فقد حصل الفعل من غير اعتقاده كون ذلك الفعل راجح المصلحة . قلنا : لا نسلم بل فيه ضرب من اللذة والمنفعة . فلم قلتم : إنه لم يوجد ذلك ؟ وبيانه من وجوه : الأول : إن البقاء على الحالة الواحدة محلول ، فهو يتحرك لتبديل الحالة المملولة . والثاني : إنه ربما اعتاد ذلك الفعل والإتيان بالأمر المعتاد لذيذ ، وتركه متعب مؤلم . والثالث : لعلة تخيل له أن له في تلك الحركة لذة ومنفعة . ثم في الحال تبدل ذلك التخيل بتخيل آخر ، وهو أن الأولى ترك تلك الحركة ، فلا جرم لم يشتغل بتحريك ذلك الإصبع ، إلا في زمان قليل . وكل هذه الاحتمالات ظاهرة جائزة
And as for the answer (1) to the second argument: it is their statement: 'Indeed, one who is deeply absorbed in thought about some important matter may sometimes have his finger move in that state, while he is heedless of that movement. Thus the action occurred without a motive (al-dā‘ī).' The answer to it: as long as that agent does not intend to move the finger, it does not move. So if they say: 'Grant that it is so, but there is in that action no benefit at all; thus the action occurred without his belief that the action is preponderant in benefit,' we say: we do not concede; rather, in it there is a kind of pleasure and benefit. So why do you claim that it does not exist? Its explanation is from several aspects: the first: remaining in a single state is boring, so he moves to change the tiresome state. The second: he may be accustomed to that action; doing what is customary is pleasurable, and abandoning it is tiresome and painful. The third: perhaps he imagines that in that movement there is pleasure and benefit. Then immediately that imagination changes to another imagination—namely, that it is better to leave that movement—so he does not engage in moving that finger except for a short time. All these possibilities are apparent and permissible.
Source pages p. 42

Commentary
This passage answers the second argument, which claimed that a person deeply absorbed in thought can move his finger unconsciously, without a motive. The author first replies that unless the agent intends the movement, it does not occur. The opponent then counters that even if the movement occurs intentionally, there is no apparent benefit, so it happens without belief in its preponderance. The author denies this, asserting that there is still some pleasure or benefit. He then gives three reasons: first, staying in one state causes boredom, so movement relieves that; second, habitual actions are pleasurable and their omission is painful; third, a fleeting imagination of benefit may occur and then quickly change. These show that a motive is always present, even if subtle or brief.

Key Terms
الداعي (al-dā‘ī, the motive)
الغفلة (al-ghafla, heedlessness)
المصلحة (al-maslaḥa, benefit)
التخيل (al-takhayyul, imagination)
اللذة (al-ladhdha, pleasure)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Automatic or unconscious actions lack a motive.
Correction: This passage shows they still have a motive, even if not consciously perceived, such as boredom relief, habit, or fleeting imagination.

Checkpoint
What is the third possible reason given for the finger movement during deep thought?
A) Boredom from staying in one state
B) Habit and the pleasure of customary actions
C) A fleeting imagination of pleasure or benefit
D) A direct command from the intellect

Key Takeaway
Even seemingly unconscious actions have a motive; the three possibilities show that a motive can be present in subtle forms such as habit or fleeting imagination.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you do something without thinking, what would it cost you to pause and ask yourself: 'What is the real motive behind this action?' Are you willing to face the answer, even if it reveals boredom or habit rather than virtue?
?
This lesson teaches that even your smallest, most unnoticed movements have a motive. If you were to apply this to your daily habits, which action would you be most ashamed to admit was driven by mere habit or fleeting imagination rather than conscious intention? What will you do about it today?
?
You often assume you know why you do things. But the opponent's argument shows we can be blind to our motives. Would you be willing to ask Allah to expose the hidden motives behind your worship, your words, and your silences—even if it humbles you? What might that reveal?

Three Causes of Movement During Sleep
Enumerates three causes of movement during sleep.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three causes of movement during sleep as identified by the author.

Core Idea
Sleep movements have definite causes, not randomness.

This passage from Al-Matalib al-Aliyah explores why a sleeping person moves. The author identifies three causes: imaginary desires or aversions, physical discomfort from lying on one side, and necessary involuntary actions like breathing. These explanations show that sleep movements are not arbitrary but have clear motivations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three causes
Read the source passage carefully and list the three causes the author gives for movement during sleep.
Each cause is introduced with a number (الأول, الثاني, الثالث).
2
Understand each cause
For each cause, think about how it leads to movement. The first involves imaginary desires, the second physical pain, the third necessity.
The author assumes sleep does not block imaginations.
3
Reflect on the implication
Consider why these causes matter: they show that sleep movements are motivated, even if the sleeper is not fully conscious of the motive.
This refutes the idea that such movements are entirely involuntary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما النائم فالسبب في حركاته وجوه : الأول : إن النائم حال نومه ، قد يرى أشياء ) من الخيالات . وربما ) (۱) يحسب تلك الخيالات إرادات وكراهات ، فتكون حركاته المختلفة بحسب تلك الخيالات . والثاني : إن طول اضطجاعه على جنبه قد يورث الألم في ذلك الجنب . وقد بينا : أن النوم لا يمنع من حصول الخيالات : فلا جرم ينقلب من أحد الجنبين إلى الثاني لدفع ذلك الألم . الثالث : إن ذلك العمل قد يكون ضرورياً ، أو شبيهاً به . مثل التنفس . وقال الشيخ أبو علي بن سينا ، في الجواب عن هذه الشبهة : إن التخيل شيء ، والشعور بحصول ذلك التخيل في الحال شيء آخر . وبقاء ذلك التخيل (۳) في الذكر بعد زواله شيء ثالث ، وليس معنا إلا أنا لا نتذكر : أنا كنا
And as for the sleeper, the cause of his movements has several aspects: the first: the sleeper, during his sleep, may see things from imaginations. And perhaps (1) he takes those imaginations to be desires and aversions, so his various movements occur according to those imaginations. The second: prolonged lying on one side may cause pain in that side. And we have explained that sleep does not prevent the occurrence of imaginations; so he turns from one side to the other to repel that pain. The third: that action may be necessary or similar to it, such as breathing. And Shaykh Abu Ali ibn Sina said, in answer to this doubt: imagination is one thing, and the awareness of the occurrence of that imagination at the moment is another thing, and the persistence of that imagination (3) in memory after its disappearance is a third thing. And we are only such that we do not remember that we were...

Commentary
This passage first explains the causes of movement during sleep. The author gives three reasons: first, the sleeper sees imaginary things and treats them as real desires or aversions, causing movement; second, discomfort from lying on one side prompts turning; third, some movements are involuntary like breathing. Then the author quotes Ibn Sina's distinction: imagination, awareness of that imagination, and its retention in memory are three separate things. The implication is that during sleep, the sleeper may have a motive (imagination) but lacks awareness or memory of it, so the movement is still motivated, though not consciously remembered. The text cuts off before completing Ibn Sina's thought.

Ibn Sina's Distinction: Imagination, Awareness, and Memory
Ibn Sina's response distinguishing three psychological faculties.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn Sina's threefold distinction between imagination, awareness, and memory, and how it explains the motivation behind sleep movements.

Core Idea
Lack of memory does not mean lack of motive.

Ibn Sina responds to a doubt: if a sleeper does not remember a motive, does that mean his movements are unmotivated? He distinguishes three separate things: imagination (the motive itself), awareness of that imagination at the moment, and memory of it later. A sleeper may have the first without the other two, so movements can still have a motive even if not remembered.

Guided Path
1
Understand the doubt
The doubt is that because a sleeper does not remember a motive, his movements might seem involuntary. Ibn Sina answers by distinguishing three aspects.
2
Learn Ibn Sina's threefold distinction
Identify the three things: imagination (takhayyul), awareness (shu'ur) of that imagination at the moment, and memory (dhikr) of it after it disappears.
The passage says these are three different things.
3
Apply the distinction to sleep
The sleeper may have the imagination (motive) but lack both awareness and memory. Thus the movement is motivated, but he does not recall it upon waking.
This resolves the doubt.

Source Text

The Contradiction in Preponderance Without a Preponderator
Razi analyzes the contradiction in the phrase 'preponderated without a preponderator'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why claiming that a capable agent preponderated one of his powers without a preponderator leads to a logical contradiction.

Core Idea
Preponderance implies an extra factor; denying it is self-contradictory.

Razi's argument begins by exposing an internal contradiction in the opposing view. He shows that the very phrase 'preponderated without a preponderator' combines two contradictory claims. This lesson unpacks that logical flaw.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation to get the flow of the argument.
Notice the structure: Razi contrasts two interpretations.
2
Identify the contradiction
Razi says 'preponderated' implies an additional concept (mafhum za'id), but 'without a preponderator' negates it. Show how this is a direct contradiction.
3
Consider the alternative
If 'preponderated' does not convey an additional concept, then the word is useless and should be omitted. That leads to a different absurdity.
This sets up the second part.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأخر، لأجل أنه اختص هذا الجانب بذلك المفهوم الزائد، فقولكم بعد هذا: إن القادر رجح أحد مقدوريه على الآخر، لا لترجح، يقتضي الجمع بين النقيضين. وذلك لأن قوله: رجح أفاد أمراً زائداً على أصل كونه قادراً، لأجله ترجح هذا الجانب على الجانب الآخر. وقوله: لا المرجح، يقتضي نفي هذا الزائد. فيثبت: أن هذا الكلام يوجب الجمع بين المرجح الزائد وبين نفيه. هذا إذا قلنا: إن قوله ترجح، يفيد مفهوماً زائداً على المفهوم من كونه قادراً. وأما إذا قلنا: إنه لا يفيد مفهوماً زائداً البتة. فحينئذ لا يكون في ذكر هذا اللفظ فائدة فوجب حذفه، والاقتصار على كونه قادراً، وحينئذ يرجع حاصل الكلام إلى أنه قادر على الفعل والترك، ونسبة قدركه إلى الطرفين على التسوية، وأنه لم يخص الجانبين بالإيقاع والترجيح، ولا بنوع من أنواع التمييز، ثم إنه إن وقع ف أحد الجانبين ينفيه. وإذا صرحنا بهذا الكلام على هذا الوجه، فإنه تقضي بديهة عقل كل أحد: أنه كلام باطل فاسد

الحجة الثانية: في بيان أن صدور الفعل عن القادر، يتوقف على الداعي: أن نقول: لنفرض أن هذا القادر كان قادراً على هذا الفعل مدة، ولم يصدر عنه ذلك الفعل. ثم بعد ذلك صدر عنه ذلك الفعل. فنقول: إما أن يقال إنه إنما حدث ذلك الفعل في ذلك الوقت دون ما قبله، لأن هذا القادر أوقعه في ذلك الوقت وما أوقعه فيما قبله. أو يقال: إنه وقع في ذلك الوقت دون ما قبله، لا لأجل أن ذلك القادر أوقعه فيه، بل وقع هو فيه لا فيه لا بسبب اصلا

أما القسم الأول. فهو يفيد مطلوبنا. وذلك لأنا عللنا وقوعه في ذلك الوقت بأن ذلك القادر أوقعه في ذلك الوقت

فهل لقولنا: أوقعه في ذلك الوقت مفهوم زائد على كونه قادراً أم لا؟ فإن كان له مفهوم زائد، فهو إنما حدث في ذلك الوقت، لأجل ذلك الوقت الزائد، فهو لم يحدث في ذلك الوقت لمجرد القادرية، بل لأجل انضمام ذلك الزائد إلى أصل القادرية. وإما أن لم يكن لقولنا: أوقعه في ذلك الوقت مفهوم زائد على أصل القادرية، كان قادراً عليه في أوقات كثيرة، ولم يتفق وقوع هذا المقدور في تلك الأوقات، وبقيت تلك القادرية إلى هذا الوقت، ووقع هذا الفعل في هذا الوقت من غير أن خصه ذلك القادر بالإيقاع في هذا الوقت، ومن غير أن خصه بالقصد إلى إيجاده وإلى تكوينه في هذا الوقت، إلا أن على هذا التقدير لا يكون وقوع ذلك الفعل في هذا الوقت منسوباً إلى ذلك القادر، بل يكون ذلك عبارة عن حدوث ذلك الشيء بنفسه، فيكون قولاً بقطع الفعل عن الفاعل، فيثبت أن القول بأن الفعل يصدر عن القادر من غير الداعي، يمنع من القول بكون الفعل فعلاً للفاعل، ويكون القادر قادراً عليه. فإن قالوا: لم لا يجوز أن يقال: إن هذا الفعل لم يقع لله القادرية في الأزمنة الماضية ووقع بها في هذا الوقت، من غير أن اختص أحد الوقتين بأمر زائد؟ فنقول: لا شك أن المفهوم من كون هذا الفعل حركة، غير المفهوم من وقوع هذه الحركة بذلك المؤثر، لأنه يصح منا أن نفهم حقيقة تلك الحركة مع الشك في وقوعها، بهذا المؤثر المعين. ونقول: ذلك المفهوم أمر مغاير لنفس هذه الحركة، ومغاير أيضاً لنفس المفهوم من القادرية، لأن كونه قادراً، قد كان حاصلا في الزمان المتقدم، مع أن المفهوم من وقوع هذه الحركة به، ما كان حاصلا. نثبت أن هذا المفهوم مفهوم زائد على ذات الأثر وعلى ذات المؤثر من حيث إنه قادر.

ولا شك أنه لولاه لما حصل هذا المقدور، فيثبت أن مجرد كون القادر قادراً لا يكفي في حصول هذا المقدور منه بعينه، بل لا بد من أمر زائد
The other, because it specified this side with that additional concept. So your statement after this: 'The capable agent preponderated one of his two objects of power over the other, not due to a preponderator,' necessitates combining the two contradictories. That is because his statement 'preponderated' conveys an additional matter beyond the essence of being capable, due to which this side preponderated over the other side. And his statement 'not due to a preponderator' necessitates the negation of this additional. So it is established that this speech necessitates combining the additional preponderator and its negation. This is if we say that his statement 'preponderated' conveys an additional concept beyond the concept from his being capable. But if we say that it does not convey any additional concept at all, then there would be no benefit in mentioning this word, so it must be omitted and one must restrict to his being capable. Then the upshot of the speech returns to that he is capable of the act and the omission, and the relation of his power to the two sides is equal, and that he did not specify the two sides by causing and preponderating, nor by any type of distinction. Then if one side occurs, it negates it. And if we state this speech in this manner, then the intuition of everyone's intellect would judge that it is invalid and corrupt speech.

The second proof: in explaining that the occurrence of the act from the capable agent depends on the motive. We say: let us assume that this capable agent was capable of this act for a period, and that act did not occur from him. Then after that, that act occurred from him. We say: either it is said that that act only happened at that time and not before, because this capable agent caused it at that time and did not cause it before. Or it is said that it occurred at that time and not before, not because that capable agent caused it, but rather it occurred itself at it, not due to any cause at all.

As for the first division, it supports our claim. That is because we explained its occurrence at that time by saying that that capable agent caused it at that time. So does our statement 'he caused it at that time' have an additional concept beyond his being capable or not? If it has an additional concept, then it only happened at that time because of that additional time, so it did not happen at that time merely because of capability, but because of the joining of that additional to the essence of capability. But if our statement 'he caused it at that time' does not have an additional concept beyond the essence of capability, then he was capable of it at many times, but the occurrence of this object of power did not happen at those times, and that capability remained until this time, and this act occurred at this time without that capable agent specifying it by causing at this time, and without him specifying it by the intention to bring it into existence and to compose it at this time. However, on this assumption, the occurrence of that act at this time would not be attributed to that capable agent, but rather that would be an expression of the thing's happening by itself, so it would be a statement cutting off the act from the agent. So it is established that the statement that the act occurs from the capable agent without the motive prevents the statement that the act is an act of the agent, and the capable agent is capable of it. If they say: why is it not permissible to say that this act did not occur by God's capability in past times, but occurred by it at this time, without one of the two times being distinguished by an additional matter? We say: there is no doubt that the concept of this act being a movement is different from the concept of the occurrence of this movement by that effecter, because it is valid for us to understand the reality of that movement while doubting its occurrence by this particular effecter. And we say: that concept is a matter different from this movement itself, and also different from the concept of capability, because his being capable was already present in the prior time, while the concept of the occurrence of this movement by it was not present. We establish that this concept is an additional concept beyond the essence of the effect and beyond the essence of the effecter insofar as he is capable.

And there is no doubt that without it this object of power would not be obtained. So it is established that mere capability of the capable is not sufficient for obtaining this object of power from him specifically, but rather an additional matter is necessary.
Source pages p. 47

Commentary
This passage is a continuation of a philosophical argument about the necessity of a preponderating factor (tarjih) or motive (da'i) for the occurrence of an act from a capable agent. It begins by addressing the interlocutor's previous statement that a capable agent preponderates one of his two objects of power over the other without a preponderator, showing that this leads to a contradiction because 'preponderated' implies an extra factor while 'without a preponderator' negates it. The author then presents a second, more detailed proof. He posits a scenario: a capable agent has the power to perform a specific act for a period but does not do so, then later performs it. The question is why the act occurs at the later time and not earlier. Two possibilities are given: either the agent caused it at that time (first division), or it occurred by itself without any cause (second division). The first division, which the author supports, implies that the occurrence is due to the agent's causing. The author then examines whether the concept of 'causing at that time' adds anything to mere capability. If it does add something, then the act happens because of that extra factor, not mere capability. If it does not add anything, then the act's occurrence at that specific time would be arbitrary, and the act would not be attributable to the agent; it would be a case of self-creation, which negates the notion of an agent altogether. A potential objection is raised: could it be that the act did not occur by divine capability in the past but does occur by it now, without any extra factor? The author responds by distinguishing between the essence of the act (e.g., a movement) and the concept of that act's occurrence by a specific effecter (the agent). These are different concepts. The agent's capability was present in the past, but the occurrence of the act by that capability was not. Hence, the concept of 'occurrence by the agent' is additional to the capability itself. The conclusion is that mere capability is insufficient; an additional matter (the preponderating factor or motive) is necessary for the act to occur specifically from that agent.

Key Terms
القادر
المقدور
الترجيح
المفهوم الزائد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that a capable agent can simply choose without any additional reason or preponderating factor.
Correction: This passage shows that the very language of 'preponderance' implies an extra factor, and denying it leads to logical absurdity.

Checkpoint
What does Razi argue about the phrase 'preponderated without a preponderator'?
A) It is a valid description of divine action.
B) It leads to a logical contradiction.
C) It correctly denies the need for a motive.
D) It is an alternative to the first proof.

Key Takeaway
The claim of preponderance without a preponderator is logically impossible; it either contradicts itself or leads to an absurd denial of agency.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever held a belief about God's actions that, if you thought it through, would lead to a logical contradiction? Are you willing to re-examine such a belief today, even if it is comfortable or familiar?
?
When you say 'God does whatever He wills,' do you ever assume that His will has no reason behind it? If so, how does Razi's argument challenge you to think more deeply about the attributes of God and their coherence?
?
Can you identify an area in your own life where you claim to have no reason for a choice, yet you clearly preferred one option over another? Would you admit that there must have been a 'preponderator' in your heart? How does that relate to your understanding of God's wisdom?

Causality and Created Things

7 min
The Second Proof: Why a Motive is Necessary for an Act
Razi presents the second proof: the scenario and two divisions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi's scenario of an act occurring at a specific time demonstrates the necessity of a preponderating motive (da'i).

Core Idea
An act occurring at a specific time requires an additional factor beyond mere capability.

Razi constructs a vivid scenario: a capable agent has the power to do an act for a long time but does not do it, then later does. Why at that specific time? He presents two possibilities: either the agent caused it then or it occurred by itself. The first leads to the necessity of an extra factor; the second denies agency altogether.

Guided Path
1
Set up the scenario
Imagine an agent capable of an act for a period but does not do it, then later does. The question is why the act occurs at that later time.
2
Two possible answers
Either the agent caused it at that time (first division) or it happened without any cause (second division).
3
Examine the first division
If the agent caused it, then 'caused at that time' must add something beyond mere capability. Otherwise, if no addition, then the act's occurrence would be arbitrary and not attributable to the agent.
4
Conclude the first division
Thus, the first division leads to the necessity of an additional motive or preponderator.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأخر، لأجل أنه اختص هذا الجانب بذلك المفهوم الزائد، فقولكم بعد هذا: إن القادر رجح أحد مقدوريه على الآخر، لا لترجح، يقتضي الجمع بين النقيضين. وذلك لأن قوله: رجح أفاد أمراً زائداً على أصل كونه قادراً، لأجله ترجح هذا الجانب على الجانب الآخر. وقوله: لا المرجح، يقتضي نفي هذا الزائد. فيثبت: أن هذا الكلام يوجب الجمع بين المرجح الزائد وبين نفيه. هذا إذا قلنا: إن قوله ترجح، يفيد مفهوماً زائداً على المفهوم من كونه قادراً. وأما إذا قلنا: إنه لا يفيد مفهوماً زائداً البتة. فحينئذ لا يكون في ذكر هذا اللفظ فائدة فوجب حذفه، والاقتصار على كونه قادراً، وحينئذ يرجع حاصل الكلام إلى أنه قادر على الفعل والترك، ونسبة قدركه إلى الطرفين على التسوية، وأنه لم يخص الجانبين بالإيقاع والترجيح، ولا بنوع من أنواع التمييز، ثم إنه إن وقع ف أحد الجانبين ينفيه. وإذا صرحنا بهذا الكلام على هذا الوجه، فإنه تقضي بديهة عقل كل أحد: أنه كلام باطل فاسد

الحجة الثانية: في بيان أن صدور الفعل عن القادر، يتوقف على الداعي: أن نقول: لنفرض أن هذا القادر كان قادراً على هذا الفعل مدة، ولم يصدر عنه ذلك الفعل. ثم بعد ذلك صدر عنه ذلك الفعل. فنقول: إما أن يقال إنه إنما حدث ذلك الفعل في ذلك الوقت دون ما قبله، لأن هذا القادر أوقعه في ذلك الوقت وما أوقعه فيما قبله. أو يقال: إنه وقع في ذلك الوقت دون ما قبله، لا لأجل أن ذلك القادر أوقعه فيه، بل وقع هو فيه لا فيه لا بسبب اصلا

أما القسم الأول. فهو يفيد مطلوبنا. وذلك لأنا عللنا وقوعه في ذلك الوقت بأن ذلك القادر أوقعه في ذلك الوقت

فهل لقولنا: أوقعه في ذلك الوقت مفهوم زائد على كونه قادراً أم لا؟ فإن كان له مفهوم زائد، فهو إنما حدث في ذلك الوقت، لأجل ذلك الوقت الزائد، فهو لم يحدث في ذلك الوقت لمجرد القادرية، بل لأجل انضمام ذلك الزائد إلى أصل القادرية. وإما أن لم يكن لقولنا: أوقعه في ذلك الوقت مفهوم زائد على أصل القادرية، كان قادراً عليه في أوقات كثيرة، ولم يتفق وقوع هذا المقدور في تلك الأوقات، وبقيت تلك القادرية إلى هذا الوقت، ووقع هذا الفعل في هذا الوقت من غير أن خصه ذلك القادر بالإيقاع في هذا الوقت، ومن غير أن خصه بالقصد إلى إيجاده وإلى تكوينه في هذا الوقت، إلا أن على هذا التقدير لا يكون وقوع ذلك الفعل في هذا الوقت منسوباً إلى ذلك القادر، بل يكون ذلك عبارة عن حدوث ذلك الشيء بنفسه، فيكون قولاً بقطع الفعل عن الفاعل، فيثبت أن القول بأن الفعل يصدر عن القادر من غير الداعي، يمنع من القول بكون الفعل فعلاً للفاعل، ويكون القادر قادراً عليه. فإن قالوا: لم لا يجوز أن يقال: إن هذا الفعل لم يقع لله القادرية في الأزمنة الماضية ووقع بها في هذا الوقت، من غير أن اختص أحد الوقتين بأمر زائد؟ فنقول: لا شك أن المفهوم من كون هذا الفعل حركة، غير المفهوم من وقوع هذه الحركة بذلك المؤثر، لأنه يصح منا أن نفهم حقيقة تلك الحركة مع الشك في وقوعها، بهذا المؤثر المعين. ونقول: ذلك المفهوم أمر مغاير لنفس هذه الحركة، ومغاير أيضاً لنفس المفهوم من القادرية، لأن كونه قادراً، قد كان حاصلا في الزمان المتقدم، مع أن المفهوم من وقوع هذه الحركة به، ما كان حاصلا. نثبت أن هذا المفهوم مفهوم زائد على ذات الأثر وعلى ذات المؤثر من حيث إنه قادر.

ولا شك أنه لولاه لما حصل هذا المقدور، فيثبت أن مجرد كون القادر قادراً لا يكفي في حصول هذا المقدور منه بعينه، بل لا بد من أمر زائد
The other, because it specified this side with that additional concept. So your statement after this: 'The capable agent preponderated one of his two objects of power over the other, not due to a preponderator,' necessitates combining the two contradictories. That is because his statement 'preponderated' conveys an additional matter beyond the essence of being capable, due to which this side preponderated over the other side. And his statement 'not due to a preponderator' necessitates the negation of this additional. So it is established that this speech necessitates combining the additional preponderator and its negation. This is if we say that his statement 'preponderated' conveys an additional concept beyond the concept from his being capable. But if we say that it does not convey any additional concept at all, then there would be no benefit in mentioning this word, so it must be omitted and one must restrict to his being capable. Then the upshot of the speech returns to that he is capable of the act and the omission, and the relation of his power to the two sides is equal, and that he did not specify the two sides by causing and preponderating, nor by any type of distinction. Then if one side occurs, it negates it. And if we state this speech in this manner, then the intuition of everyone's intellect would judge that it is invalid and corrupt speech.

The second proof: in explaining that the occurrence of the act from the capable agent depends on the motive. We say: let us assume that this capable agent was capable of this act for a period, and that act did not occur from him. Then after that, that act occurred from him. We say: either it is said that that act only happened at that time and not before, because this capable agent caused it at that time and did not cause it before. Or it is said that it occurred at that time and not before, not because that capable agent caused it, but rather it occurred itself at it, not due to any cause at all.

As for the first division, it supports our claim. That is because we explained its occurrence at that time by saying that that capable agent caused it at that time. So does our statement 'he caused it at that time' have an additional concept beyond his being capable or not? If it has an additional concept, then it only happened at that time because of that additional time, so it did not happen at that time merely because of capability, but because of the joining of that additional to the essence of capability. But if our statement 'he caused it at that time' does not have an additional concept beyond the essence of capability, then he was capable of it at many times, but the occurrence of this object of power did not happen at those times, and that capability remained until this time, and this act occurred at this time without that capable agent specifying it by causing at this time, and without him specifying it by the intention to bring it into existence and to compose it at this time. However, on this assumption, the occurrence of that act at this time would not be attributed to that capable agent, but rather that would be an expression of the thing's happening by itself, so it would be a statement cutting off the act from the agent. So it is established that the statement that the act occurs from the capable agent without the motive prevents the statement that the act is an act of the agent, and the capable agent is capable of it. If they say: why is it not permissible to say that this act did not occur by God's capability in past times, but occurred by it at this time, without one of the two times being distinguished by an additional matter? We say: there is no doubt that the concept of this act being a movement is different from the concept of the occurrence of this movement by that effecter, because it is valid for us to understand the reality of that movement while doubting its occurrence by this particular effecter. And we say: that concept is a matter different from this movement itself, and also different from the concept of capability, because his being capable was already present in the prior time, while the concept of the occurrence of this movement by it was not present. We establish that this concept is an additional concept beyond the essence of the effect and beyond the essence of the effecter insofar as he is capable.

And there is no doubt that without it this object of power would not be obtained. So it is established that mere capability of the capable is not sufficient for obtaining this object of power from him specifically, but rather an additional matter is necessary.
Source pages p. 47

Commentary
This passage is a continuation of a philosophical argument about the necessity of a preponderating factor (tarjih) or motive (da'i) for the occurrence of an act from a capable agent. It begins by addressing the interlocutor's previous statement that a capable agent preponderates one of his two objects of power over the other without a preponderator, showing that this leads to a contradiction because 'preponderated' implies an extra factor while 'without a preponderator' negates it. The author then presents a second, more detailed proof. He posits a scenario: a capable agent has the power to perform a specific act for a period but does not do so, then later performs it. The question is why the act occurs at the later time and not earlier. Two possibilities are given: either the agent caused it at that time (first division), or it occurred by itself without any cause (second division). The first division, which the author supports, implies that the occurrence is due to the agent's causing. The author then examines whether the concept of 'causing at that time' adds anything to mere capability. If it does add something, then the act happens because of that extra factor, not mere capability. If it does not add anything, then the act's occurrence at that specific time would be arbitrary, and the act would not be attributable to the agent; it would be a case of self-creation, which negates the notion of an agent altogether. A potential objection is raised: could it be that the act did not occur by divine capability in the past but does occur by it now, without any extra factor? The author responds by distinguishing between the essence of the act (e.g., a movement) and the concept of that act's occurrence by a specific effecter (the agent). These are different concepts. The agent's capability was present in the past, but the occurrence of the act by that capability was not. Hence, the concept of 'occurrence by the agent' is additional to the capability itself. The conclusion is that mere capability is insufficient; an additional matter (the preponderating factor or motive) is necessary for the act to occur specifically from that agent.

Key Terms
القادر
المقدور
الداعي
المفهوم الزائد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the capability (qudra) alone is sufficient for an act to occur at any time.
Correction: Razi shows that if capability alone were sufficient, the act would have occurred at all times when the agent was capable, or the specific timing would be inexplicable without an extra factor.

Checkpoint
According to the second proof, what does the first division imply about the necessity of a motive?
A) It shows that capability alone is sufficient.
B) It shows that a motive is unnecessary.
C) It leads to the conclusion that an extra factor is required for the act's occurrence at a specific time.
D) It proves that acts occur by themselves.

Key Takeaway
The occurrence of an act at a specific time from a capable agent cannot be explained by mere capability; an additional preponderating factor is logically required.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Razi argues that without a specific motive, an act would not truly be attributed to you. Think of a recent action you did—what was the 'additional factor' beyond your mere ability that made you do it at that moment? Are you honest with yourself about your real motives?
?
If your capability alone could explain all your actions, then your choices would be meaningless. How often do you act impulsively without examining the 'motive' behind your deeds? What one motive have you been avoiding acknowledging today?
?
The proof shows that an act without a motive severs the act from the agent. Consider a habit you have that you feel is out of your control. Is it truly an act of yours if it lacks a conscious motive? What would it take to reclaim that act by intentionally choosing a motive?

Causality and Created Things
intermediate

The Additional Concept: Answering the Objection
Razi responds to an objection and reinforces the need for an additional concept.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the concept of 'occurrence by the agent' is distinct from both the act's essence and the agent's capability, and why this proves the necessity of a preponderating factor.

Core Idea
The occurrence of an act by a specific agent is an additional concept beyond the act itself and the agent's power.

An objector suggests that the act could occur at the later time purely by the same capability that existed earlier, without any extra factor. Razi responds by distinguishing three concepts: the act's essence, the agent's capability, and the act's occurrence by the agent. The latter is an additional concept that was absent earlier, proving that an extra factor must be present.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
The objector says: why can't the act occur now by the same capability that was present before, without any extra factor?
2
Razi's response: three distinct concepts
Razi distinguishes: the act itself (e.g., movement), the capability, and the occurrence of that act by the agent. The occurrence by the agent is not the same as the act or the capability.
3
Why the occurrence concept is additional
The capability existed in the past, but the occurrence by that agent did not. So the concept of 'occurrence by the agent' is separate and additional.
4
Conclusion
Thus, mere capability is insufficient; an additional preponderating factor (the motive) is necessary for the act to occur specifically from that agent.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The question is why the act occurs at the later time and not earlier. Two possibilities are given: either the agent caused it at that time (first division), or it occurred by itself without any cause (second division). The first division, which the author supports, implies that the occurrence is due to the agent's causing. The author then examines whether the concept of 'causing at that time' adds anything to mere capability. If it does add something, then the act happens because of that extra factor, not mere capability. If it does not add anything, then the act's occurrence at that specific time would be arbitrary, and the act would not be attributable to the agent; it would be a case of self-creation, which negates the notion of an agent altogether. A potential objection is raised: could it be that the act did not occur by divine capability in the past but does occur by it now, without any extra factor? The author responds by distinguishing between the essence of the act (e.g., a movement) and the concept of that act's occurrence by a specific effecter (the agent). These are different concepts. The agent's capability was present in the past, but the occurrence of the act by that capability was not. Hence, the concept of 'occurrence by the agent' is additional to the capability itself. The conclusion is that mere capability is insufficient; an additional matter (the preponderating factor or motive) is necessary for the act to occur specifically from that agent.

Key Terms
المفهوم الزائد
المؤثر
القادرية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might conflate the existence of capability with the actual occurrence of an act by that capability.
Correction: Razi shows that capability is a disposition, while the actual occurrence of an act by that capability is a separate state requiring a preponderating factor.

Checkpoint
How does Razi demonstrate that the concept of 'occurrence of the act by the agent' is additional?
A) By showing that the act could not exist without the agent.
B) By pointing out that the capacity existed in the past but the occurrence did not.
C) By asserting that the agent's power is constant.
D) By quoting scripture.

Key Takeaway
Mere capability does not entail actual occurrence; an additional preponderating factor is needed for the specific act to be attributed to the agent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your own life, do you sometimes assume that because you have the ability to do something (like pray, be patient, or change a habit), that ability alone is sufficient to actually make it happen? Where have you neglected the 'additional matter'—the genuine motive or decisive intention—that turns capability into action? What is one specific action today that you can move from mere potential to actual occurrence by attending to that missing factor?
?
This lesson teaches that God's power alone does not necessitate an act without an additional preponderating factor. How does this change your understanding of divine will and your own dua? Do you approach God as if His power automatically guarantees your request, or do you humbly seek His motive and wisdom? What does this mean for a specific situation you are waiting for God to resolve?

The Necessity of a Preponderator for Acts at Specific Times
Logical argument with objection and response on the necessity of al-dāʿī.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why mere capability is insufficient to explain why an act occurs at a particular time, and why an additional motive (al-dāʿī) is necessary.

Core Idea
Capability alone cannot specify an act to a particular time; a preponderator is essential.

In this passage, the author tackles a subtle question: if a capable agent can do something at many times, what makes it happen at one specific time? The answer, he argues, cannot be mere capability, because each time is equally possible. He demonstrates that an additional factor—the motive or preponderator (al-dāʿī)—must be present. This reasoning is crucial for understanding how agents cause events in time.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the argument's logical structure.
2
Identify the two conditional branches
The author starts with two scenarios: whether 'occurrence at this time' adds something to mere capability or not. Understand the consequence of each.
The second scenario leads to an absurdity.
3
Examine the objection and the response
After showing the second scenario fails, an objection is raised: why not say capability simply works now without addition? The author responds by distinguishing three concepts (act, occurrence-by-effecter, capability).
Focus on why occurrence-by-effecter is a distinct concept.
4
Conclude the argument
The response shows that a real additional concept is needed, which is al-dāʿī. Confirm that mere capability is insufficient.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كان له مفهوم زائد، فهو إنما حدث في ذلك الوقت، لأجل ذلك الوقت الزائد، فهو لم يحدث في ذلك الوقت لمجرد القادرية، بل لأجل انضمام ذلك الزائد إلى أصل القادرية. وإما أن لم يكن لقولنا: أوقعه في ذلك الوقت مفهوم زائد على أصل القادرية، كان قادراً عليه في أوقات كثيرة، ولم يتفق وقوع هذا المقدور في تلك الأوقات، وبقيت تلك القادرية إلى هذا الوقت، ووقع هذا الفعل في هذا الوقت من غير أن خصه ذلك القادر بالإيقاع في هذا الوقت، ومن غير أن خصه بالقصد إلى إيجاده وإلى تكوينه في هذا الوقت، إلا أن على هذا التقدير لا يكون وقوع ذلك الفعل في هذا الوقت منسوباً إلى ذلك القادر، بل يكون ذلك عبارة عن حدوث ذلك الشيء بنفسه، فيكون قولاً بقطع الفعل عن الفاعل، فيثبت أن القول بأن الفعل يصدر عن القادر من غير الداعي، يمنع من القول بكون الفعل فعلاً للفاعل، ويكون القادر قادراً عليه. فإن قالوا: لم لا يجوز أن يقال: إن هذا الفعل لم يقع لهله القادرية في الأزمنة الماضية ووقع بها في هذا الوقت، من غير أن اختص أحد الوقتين بأمر زائد؟ فنقول: لا شك أن المفهوم من كون هذا الفعل حركة، غير المفهوم من وقوع هذه الحركة بذلك المؤثر، لأنه يصح منا أن نفهم حقيقة تلك الحركة مع الشك في وقوعها بهذا المؤثر المعين. ونقول: ذلك المفهوم أمر مغاير لنفس هذه الحركة، ومغاير أيضاً لنفس المفهوم من القادرية، لأن كونه قادراً، قد كان حاصلا في الزمان المتقدم، مع أن المفهوم من وقوع هذه الحركة به، ما كان حاصلا. فنثبت أن هذا المفهوم مفهوم زائد على ذات الأثر وعلى ذات المؤثر من حيث إنه قادر. ولا شك أنه لولاه لما حصل هذا المقدور، فيثبت أن مجرد كون القادر قادراً لا يكفي في حصول هذا المقدور منه بعينه، بل لا بد من أمر زائد وذلك هو الداعي. والله أعلم.
If there is an additional concept, then it occurred at that time because of that additional time; so it did not occur at that time merely because of capability, but because of the joining of that addition to the basis of capability. And if there is no additional concept beyond capability for our saying 'He made it occur at that time', then he would be capable of it at many times, and it would not happen that this predetermined thing occurred at those times, and that capability would remain until this time, and this act would occur at this time without that capable one specifying it to occur at this time, and without him specifying it by intending to bring it into existence and to form it at this time. However, on this assumption, the occurrence of that act at this time would not be attributed to that capable one, but rather it would be an expression of the event of that thing by itself, making it a statement of cutting the act off from the agent. Thus it is established that the statement that the act proceeds from the capable one without the motive (al-dāʿī) prevents the statement that the act is an act of the agent, while the capable one is capable of it. If they say: Why is it not permissible to say that this act did not occur [by?] the capability in past times and occurred by it in this time, without either time being distinguished by an additional matter? We say: There is no doubt that the concept of this act being a motion is different from the concept of this motion occurring by that effecter (al-muʾaththir), because we can understand the reality of that motion while doubting its occurrence by this specific effecter. And we say: That concept is a matter different from this motion itself, and also different from the concept of capability itself, because his being capable was present in past time, while the concept of the occurrence of this motion by him was not present. So we establish that this concept is an additional concept beyond the essence of the effect and beyond the essence of the effecter insofar as he is capable. And there is no doubt that without it this predetermined thing would not occur; thus it is established that mere being of the capable one as capable is not sufficient for the occurrence of this predetermined thing from him specifically; rather, an additional matter is necessary, and that is the motive (al-dāʿī). And God knows best.
Source pages p. 48

Commentary
This passage presents a logical argument and an objection-response regarding the necessity of an additional factor (الداعي, al-dāʿī) for an act to occur at a specific time from a capable agent. The author first argues that if the concept of 'occurrence at a specific time' adds something to mere capability, then the act occurs at that time only because of that addition, not because of capability alone. If it adds nothing, then the capable agent could have performed the act at many times, but it only occurs at one time without any specification by him; this would mean the act happens by itself, severing the act from the agent, which is impossible. Then an objection is raised: why not say the act simply did not occur with capability in past times and now occurs with it, without any additional factor? The author replies by distinguishing three concepts: the act itself (e.g., motion), the occurrence of that act by the effecter, and the capability. The concept of occurrence-by-the-effecter is additional to both the act and the capability, because one can understand the act while doubting its occurrence by a particular agent, and capability existed before without that occurrence. Thus, a real additional concept is required, and that is the motive (الداعي). The passage concludes that mere capability is insufficient; a preponderator is necessary.

Key Terms
الداعي
القادرية
المؤثر
المقدور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Capability alone is sufficient for an act to occur at a specific time without any additional motive.
Correction: The author shows that without an additional preponderator, the act would not be attributable to the agent, implying it would occur by itself, which is false.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what would be the consequence if the concept of 'occurrence at this time' added nothing to mere capability?
A) The act would be necessarily caused by the agent.
B) The act would occur at many times simultaneously.
C) The act would be disconnected from the agent, happening by itself.
D) The agent would lose his capability.

Key Takeaway
Mere capability cannot determine the specific timing of an act; an additional motive (الداعي) is required.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a time you postponed an action you were fully capable of doing. What was the missing motive? How does this lesson reveal that your capability was never the issue, but rather the absence of a preponderating motive? Will you continue to rely on capability alone, or will you seek to strengthen your motives?
?
This passage argues that without a specific motive, an act cannot be attributed to the agent. Examine your own actions: how many of your daily deeds are truly chosen with a conscious motive, and how many are merely habitual? Are you comfortable attributing those habitual acts to yourself, or does this lesson challenge your sense of agency?
?
If God's power is absolute, does He still require a motive to act? Reflect on the implication: if even the Creator's acts follow this principle, how much more should you examine the motives behind your own worship and obedience? Is your prayer today driven by a genuine motive or mere routine?

The Uniformity of Possibility: A Universal Principle
Sanusi presents the uniform principle and its application to agreed cases.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the principle of possibility and permissibility is uniform and requires a preponderator in all cases, using the examples of substance and possible being.

Core Idea
The same logic of possibility demands a preponderator everywhere without exception.

Sanusi begins the third proof by establishing a crucial principle: the concept of possibility and permissibility is one and the same. If it requires a preponderator in one scenario, it must in all. He then reminds his opponents of two agreed cases to build his argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the principle statement
Read the opening sentence: 'إن قضية الإمكان والجواز قضية واحدة' and its implication for requiring a preponderator consistently.
Notice the logical disjunction: either it requires a preponderator in all cases or in none.
2
Examine the agreed examples
Sanusi uses the examples of a substance (جوهر) in a location and a possible being (ممكن) for existence. In both, opponents agree a preponderator is needed.
These are uncontested cases, so they serve as common ground.
3
Apply to the capable agent
The argument then applies the same logic to capability (قدرة): since it is equally open to act and omission, a preponderator is required.
This is the core move of the proof.
4
Reflect on uniformity
Consider the final statement that there is no difference between these cases in the category of possibility and permissibility.
This underscores why the principle must be uniform.
