Objection to the Proof and Its Refutation
Razi presents an objection to his proof and refutes it by showing that preponderance of one side necessitates preponderatedness of the other.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that non-existence remains preponderant despite motivation, and Razi's refutation by necessity of preponderatedness.

Core Idea
Non-existence cannot remain preponderant if existence is preponderated; the two contradictories force each other.

This passage presents an objection to Razi's first proof. The objector argues that when motivation preponderates existence, non-existence remains as an original continuous state and thus remains preponderant as well. This would mean both sides are preponderant, which undermines the necessity claimed by Razi. Razi responds by analyzing the nature of preponderance: either the motivation truly makes existence preponderant or it does not. If it does, then non-existence must become preponderated because the two contradictories are mutually opposed. Thus the objection fails and the proof stands.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
The objector claims that non-existence is an original state that continues, so it remains preponderant even when existence becomes preponderant.
This is a subtle point about the persistence of non-existence.
2
Identify the objector's assumption
The objector assumes that non-existence can be preponderant independently of the preponderance of existence.
3
Read Razi's answer
Razi uses a dilemma: either the motivation truly preponderates existence or it doesn't. If it doesn't, then it's not a motivation. If it does, then non-existence must become preponderated because the two contradictories are opposed.
This is the crux of the refutation.
4
Understand the concept of mutual opposition
Razi invokes the principle that the two contradictories (action and inaction) are mutually exclusive and exhaustive. Preponderance of one necessarily implies preponderatedness of the other.
This reaffirms the conclusion of the first proof.
5
Confirm the conclusion
Razi concludes that when one side is preponderant, the other is necessarily preponderated, so the objection fails. The action becomes obligatory.
This solidifies the entire argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعلم أن من الناس من قال : إن عند حصول الداعي يصير الفعل أولى بالوقوع . لكنه لا يبلغ تلك الأولوية إلى حد الوجوب . واعلم أن هذا القول باطل ويدل عليه وجوه :
الحجة الأولى : إن الفعل والترك قبل حصول الداعي كانا في حد التساوي . وقد بينا أنه ما دام هذا الاستواء يكون باقياً ، فإنه يمتنع حصول الفعل فإذا حصلت الداعية الموجبة لرجحان أحد الطرفين ، فعند هذه الحالة صار الطرف الثاني مرجوحاً . والمرجوح أضعف حالا من المتساوي ، ولما كان عند حصول الاستواء : ممتنع الحصول، مستحيل الوقوع، فعند حصول المرجوحية كان أولى بالامتناع. وإذا صار أحد الطرفين ممتنع الوقوع، صار الطرف الآخر واجب الوقوع . ضرورة أن الخروج من طرف النقيضين محال ، فيثبت بهذا البرهان . أن الفعل يصير واجب الوقوع عند حصول الداعي
فإن قيل : إنه إذا حصلت الداعية المرجحة لجانب وجود الفعل ، صار جانب وجوده راجحاً. وهذا لا يقتضي ضرورة جانب العدم مرجوحاً ، لأن ذلك العدم عدم باقي مستمر والعلم الباقي المستمر لا يكون التأثير فيه حال بقائه على ذلك العدم المستمر ، فيثبت أن العدم الأصلي نفي كما كان حال قيام هذه الداعية المرجحة لجانب الوجود . وإذا كان الأمر كذلك امتنع ضرورة ذلك العدم مرجوحاً ، بل بقي ذلك العدم راجحاً ، كما كان ، نظراً إلى أن الأصل في كل ثابت : بقاؤه على ما كان ، وصار جانب الوجود أيضاً راجحاً ، بسبب أن المقتضى لحصول هذا الرجحان قد حصل .
والجواب : أن نقول : إن عند حصول الداعية المقتضية للوجود . إما أن يكون قد ترجح جانب الوجود أو لم يترجح البتة ، فإن لم يترجح البتة امتنع كون تلك الداعية داعية إلى الفعل، لكنا قد فرضناها داعية إلى الفعل. هذا خلف . وإن ترجح جانب الوجود ، وجب أن يصير جانب العدم مرجوحاً لا محالة بالنسبة إلى جانب الوجود ، لأن النقيضين لما تقابلا وتعاندا ، كان رجحان أحد الجانبين يقتضي مرجوحية الجانب الآخر بعينه ، فيثبت أن الجانب الأول ، لما صار راجحاً ، صار الجانب الثاني مرجوحاً . لا محالة . وعند هذا يحصل المطلوب
Know that among people there is one who says: When motivation (al-dā'ī) occurs, the action becomes more likely to occur (awlā bi l-wuqū'), but this likelihood does not reach the level of obligation (al-wujūb). Know that this statement is false, and it is indicated by proofs. The first proof: The action and inaction prior to the occurrence of motivation were at the level of equality (al-tasāwī). We have shown that as long as this equality remains, the occurrence of the action is impossible (mumtani'). When the motivating factor (al-dā'iyya al-mūjiba) for the preponderance of one side occurs, at that point the other side becomes preponderated (marjūḥ). The preponderated is weaker than the equal. Since at the occurrence of equality the action was impossible, at the occurrence of preponderance it is more deserving of impossibility. When one side becomes impossible of occurrence, the other side becomes necessary of occurrence (wājib al-wuqū'), necessarily because exit from one of the two contradictories (naqīḍayn) is impossible. Thus by this proof it is established that the action becomes necessary of occurrence when motivation occurs. If it is said: When the motivating factor preponderating the side of existence occurs, the side of existence becomes preponderant (rājiḥ). This does not necessarily necessitate that the side of non-existence becomes preponderated, because that non-existence is a continuing original non-existence, and a continuing non-existence is not affected in its state of continuation by that motivating factor preponderating the side of existence. Thus it is established that the original non-existence remains as it was at the time of existence of this motivating factor preponderating the side of existence. If this is so, it is impossible that this non-existence becomes preponderated necessarily; rather, that non-existence remains preponderant, as it was, considering that the principle for every established thing is its remaining as it was. And the side of existence also became preponderant because the requisite for obtaining this preponderance has occurred. The answer: We say: When the motivating factor that necessitates existence occurs, either the side of existence has become preponderant or it has not become preponderant at all. If it has not become preponderant at all, then it is impossible for that factor to be motivating to action, but we have posited it as motivating to action — this is a contradiction (khalaf). If the side of existence has become preponderant, then it is necessary that the side of non-existence becomes preponderated necessarily relative to the side of existence, because the two contradictories, in their mutual opposition and repugnance, the preponderance of one side necessitates the preponderatedness of the other side precisely. Thus it is established that when the first side became preponderant, the second side became preponderated necessarily. And thereby the desired is obtained.
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of a claim about the nature of motivation, followed by a proof and an objection-and-answer. The author first states the false view: some say that when a sufficient motivation (dā'ī) exists, the action becomes more likely but not obligatory. The author declares this false and presents a first proof. The proof begins with the premise that before motivation, action and inaction are equal (tasāwī). It has been established that when equal, action is impossible. When motivation arises to preponderate one side, the other side becomes preponderated (marjūḥ), which is weaker than equal. Since the equal was impossible, the preponderated is even more impossible. Therefore, the preponderated side becomes impossible of occurrence, and by the law of excluded middle (the two contradictories), the other side becomes necessary. Thus, action becomes obligatory when motivation occurs. Then an objection is raised: The non-existence is an original continuing state, and the motivation only affects existence, not non-existence. So non-existence remains preponderant, and existence also becomes preponderant, leading to two preponderants, not necessarily obligation. The author answers: either the motivation causes preponderance of existence or not. If not, it is not a motivation—contradiction. If it does cause preponderance, then non-existence must become preponderated relative to existence, because the two contradictories are opposed. So the second side becomes preponderated, and thus the first side becomes necessary. This fulfills the claim.

Razi's Second Proof: Necessity of Action After Sufficient Motivation
Razi presents a second proof that sufficient motivation necessitates the action.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's second proof that when sufficient motivation is present, non-existence of the action becomes impossible.

Core Idea
If the cause is sufficient, non-existence becomes impossible.

Razi continues his defense of causal necessity with a second proof. He sets up a dilemma: after sufficient motivation, either non-existence is impossible or it is not. The proof aims to show that the first option is the only rational conclusion. This lesson walks through the initial steps of that proof.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main claim
Read the first line carefully: Razi aims to show that when the preponderating motive occurs, the action becomes necessary (wājib al-wuqū').
The proof uses the terms 'preponderance' (rujhān) and 'effect' (athar).
2
Understand the first possibility
Razi posits: after preponderance on the side of existence, either non-existence is impossible (mumtani') or not. If impossible, then existence is necessary—this is the desired conclusion.
3
Examine the second possibility
If non-existence is not impossible, then we assume the effect can occur at one time and not at another. This distinction needs a cause.
The proof will later show this leads to an infinite regress.
4
Recognize the need for an extra condition
The distinction between occurrence and non-occurrence either depends on an additional condition (qayd zā'id) or not. The passage cuts off here, but the argument aims to rule out both options, forcing the first possibility.
The full proof is incomplete in the source, but the logical structure is clear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية : في بيان أن عند حصول الداعية المرجحة ، يصير الفعل واجب الوقوع :
أن نقول : إن عند حصول الرجحان في جانب الوجود ، إما أن يكون ممتنعاً أو لا يكون . فإن كان ممتنعاً ، فهذا هو المطلوب ، لأن كل ما يمتنع عدمه ، فقد ثبت وجوب وجوده ، وإن لم يمتنع فنقول : كل ما يكون ممتنعاً لم يلزم من فرض وقوعه محال ، فلنفرض عند حصول ذلك الرجحان ذلك الأثر تارة واقعاً ، وتارة غير واقع ، فتميز وقت الوقوع، عن وقت اللاوقوع. إما أن يتوقف على انضمام قيد زائد إليه لأجله صار أولى بالوقوع، أو لا يتوقف.
The second proof: To show that when the preponderating motivating factor occurs, the action becomes necessary of occurrence: We say: When preponderance (al-rujhān) occurs on the side of existence, either non-existence is impossible (mumtani') or not. If it is impossible, then this is the desired conclusion, because everything whose non-existence is impossible has its existence established as necessary. And if it is not impossible, then we say: Everything that is not impossible, its occurrence is not necessarily absurd (muḥāl). So let us suppose, at the occurrence of that preponderance, that effect sometimes occurs and sometimes does not occur. Then the time of occurrence is distinguished from the time of non-occurrence. Either this distinction depends on the addition of an extra condition (qayd zā'id), due to which it becomes more likely to occur, or it does not depend.
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This passage is a second proof for the same conclusion: when a preponderating motivation occurs, the action becomes obligatory. The author sets up a dichotomy: after preponderance on the side of existence, is non-existence impossible or not? If non-existence is impossible, then existence is necessary—that is the desired result. If non-existence is not impossible, then we consider the effect (the action) that results from the preponderance. We suppose it occurs at one time and does not occur at another. This distinction between occurrence and non-occurrence must have a cause. Either it depends on an additional condition (qayd zā'id) that makes the action more likely, or it does not. The proof is incomplete in the available text, but the author seems to be setting up a regress or an argument that such a condition would lead to an infinite chain, thus the non-existence must be impossible. The passage ends mid-argument, so the full proof is not given. The intended point is that if there is no additional condition, then the motivation alone would always produce the action, contradicting the possibility of non-occurrence. If there is an additional condition, then the same reasoning applies to that condition, leading to an infinite regress, which is impossible. Thus, non-existence must be impossible, confirming obligation.

Key Terms
ممتنع
واجب
رجحان
قيد زائد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument is about free will and moral responsibility.
Correction: The argument is about logical necessity: if the cause is sufficient, the effect must follow. It does not address moral freedom.

Checkpoint
What is the first possibility considered in the second proof?
A) That non-existence is impossible after preponderance.
B) That the action is merely likely.
C) That motivation is irrelevant.
D) That the agent can choose freely.

Key Takeaway
Razi's second proof establishes that once sufficient motivation is present, non-existence of the action is logically impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Are you allowing an 'extra condition' to excuse your inaction? This week, identify one such act and commit to doing it immediately after the motivation arises, trusting that the proof demands necessity.
?
Razi argues that if non-existence were possible after sufficient motivation, an infinite regress of conditions would follow. Think of a recurring sin in your life: what 'extra condition' do you keep waiting for before you stop? How can you remove that condition and treat your intention as sufficient?
?
The proof forces a conclusion: once preponderance occurs, the action must happen. Is there a relationship with Allah that you keep saying you intend to improve, yet never act? What would it cost you today to treat that intention as sufficient motivation and act without delay?

The Logical Implications of the Preponderating Motive
Presents an objection and answer on the preponderating motive

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the presence of a preponderating motive logically necessitates the preponderance of one side and the non-preponderance of its opposite, and why the objection that the opposite remains preponderant is self-defeating.

Core Idea
A genuine motive for action cannot exist without making its opposite non-preponderant.

This passage presents a classic objection in kalam: if a motive for existence arises, does the opposite non-existence lose its preponderance? Sanusi shows that assuming a motive that does not actually make its side preponderant leads to a contradiction. The answer hinges on the mutual exclusivity of contradictories.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to understand the objection and the logical dilemma presented. Note the structure: objection, then answer with two branches.
Pay attention to the terms 'preponderating motive', 'preponderant', 'non-preponderant'.
2
Identify the objection
The objection claims that even when the motive for existence is present, the non-existence remains preponderant because of the principle that an established thing remains as it was.
3
Follow the answer's dilemma
The answer presents two possibilities: either the side of existence becomes preponderant or it does not. If it does not, the motive is impossible. If it does, the opposite must become non-preponderant due to mutual exclusivity.
This is a reductio ad absurdum (khulf).
4
Understand the key terms
Focus on 'dā'iya murjiha' (preponderating motive), 'tarajjuḥ' (preponderance), 'marjūḥ' (non-preponderant), 'naqīḍān' (contradictories). These are central to the argument.
5
Reflect on the logical necessity
Consider why the principle of mutual exclusivity forces the opposite to become non-preponderant. This demonstrates the rational necessity in causal judgments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قيام هذه الداعية المرجحة لجانب الوجود . وإذا كان الأمر كذلك امتنع ضرورة ذلك العدم مرجوحاً ، بل بقي ذلك العدم راجحاً ، كما كان ، نظراً إلى أن الأصل في كل ثابت : بقاؤه على ما كان ، وصار جانب الوجود أيضاً راجحاً، أن المقتضى الحصول هذا الرجحان قد حصل . والجواب : أن نقول : إن عند حصول الداعية المقتضية للوجود . إما أن يكون قد ترجح جانب الوجود أو لم يترجح البتة ، فإن لم يترجح البتة امتنع كون تلك الداعية داعية إلى الفعل، لكنا قد فرضناها داعية إلى الفعل. هذا خلف . وإن ترجح جانب الوجود ، وجب أن يصير جانب العدم مرجوحاً لا محالة بالنسبة إلى جانب الوجود ، لأن النقيضين لما تقابلا وتعاندا ، كان رجحان أحد الجانبين يقتضي مرجوحية الجانب الآخر بعينه ، فيثبت أن الجانب الأول ، لما صار راجحاً ، صار الجانب الثاني مرجوحاً لا محالة . وعند هذا يحصل المطلوب.
The existence of this preponderating motive for the side of existence. And if this is the case, then necessarily that non-existence is not preponderated, rather that non-existence remains preponderant, as it was, considering that the original rule for every established thing is its remaining as it was, and the side of existence also became preponderant, because the requirement for obtaining this preponderance has obtained. And the answer: that we say: When the motive that necessitates existence occurs, either the side of existence has become preponderant or it has not become preponderant at all. If it has not become preponderant at all, then it is impossible for that motive to be a motive to action, but we have assumed it to be a motive to action. This is a contradiction. And if the side of existence becomes preponderant, then it is necessary that the side of non-existence becomes non-preponderant necessarily relative to the side of existence, because since the two contradictories are opposed and mutually exclusive, the preponderance of one side necessitates the non-preponderance of the other side exactly. So it is established that when the first side becomes preponderant, the second side becomes non-preponderant necessarily. And at this point the desired result is obtained.
Source pages p. 57

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer regarding the implications of the preponderating motive (al-dā'iya al-murjiha). The objection claims that even when the motive for existence is present, the non-existence does not become non-preponderant (marjūḥ); rather it remains preponderant (rājiḥ) because the original rule for every established thing is its persistence. The answer uses a logical dilemma: either the side of existence becomes preponderant or not. If it does not become preponderant, then the motive cannot be a motive to action—contradicting the assumption. If it does become preponderant, then the side of non-existence must become non-preponderant because the two contradictories (naqīḍān) are mutually exclusive; the preponderance of one necessitates the non-preponderance of the other. Thus the required conclusion is reached.

Key Terms
داعية مرجحة
ترجح
مرجوح
نقيضين
خلف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the presence of a motive does not affect the status of the opposite, so the opposite remains preponderant even when the motive is present.
Correction: The answer shows that if the motive is genuine, it necessarily makes its side preponderant, and due to mutual exclusivity, the opposite becomes non-preponderant. If it did not, the motive would be self-contradictory.

Checkpoint
According to the answer, what is the consequence if the side of existence does not become preponderant when the motive occurs?
A) The motive is still valid.
B) The motive cannot be a motive to action.
C) The side of non-existence becomes non-preponderant.
D) The motive is unnecessary.

Key Takeaway
The preponderance of one side of a contradiction necessarily entails the non-preponderance of the other, otherwise the motive would be self-contradictory.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
If your motive for obedience is real, then disobedience should feel non-preponderant. Does it? If not, what does that reveal about the state of your heart, and what will you do about it today?

intermediate

The Necessity of the Act with a Preponderating Motive
Demonstrates that when a preponderating motive exists, the act is necessarily realized

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the existence of a preponderating motive necessitates the act, and how the argument eliminates alternatives through the impossibility of preponderance without a preponderator.

Core Idea
When a motive fully tips the scales, the act must occur—otherwise specification is arbitrary.

In this lesson, we examine a sophisticated proof from Imam Razi that demonstrates the necessary connection between a complete motive and the act. The argument proceeds by considering two possibilities for the preponderance of existence over non-existence, showing that both lead to the conclusion that the act is necessary. This proof deepens our understanding of causality and rational judgments in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the literal translation. Get a feel for the argument's flow without dissecting it yet.
2
Follow the conditional structure
The proof first splits into: the preponderance is either impossible or not impossible. Trace how each branch is handled. If impossible, the act is necessary. If not impossible, a further analysis unfolds.
Notice the use of 'either...or' (إما أن يكون... أو لا يكون).
3
Understand the reductio ad absurdum
When the preponderance is possible, the proof considers whether occurrence depends on an extra restriction. If yes, contradiction arises because the act was impossible yet judged more deserving. If no, then specification of one time over the other would be a preponderance without a preponderator, which is impossible.
This shows that the only consistent scenario is the first branch: the act is necessary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية : في بيان أن عند حصول الداعية المرجحة ، يصير الفعل واجب الوقوع : أن نقول : إن عند حصول الرجحان في جانب الوجود ، إما أن يكون ممتنعاً أو لا يكون . فإن كان ممتنعاً ، فهذا هو المطلوب ، لأن كل ما يمتنع العدم عدمه ، فقد ثبت وجوب وجوده ، وإن لم يمتنع فنقول : كل ما لا يكون ممتنعاً لم يلزم من فرض وقوعه محال ، فلنفرض عند حصول ذلك الرجحان ذلك الأثر تارة واقعاً ، وتارة غير واقع ، فتميز وقت الوقوع، عن وقت اللاوقوع. إما أن يتوقف على انضمام قيد زائد إليه لأجله صار أولى بالوقوع، أو لا يتوقف. فإن توقف الوقوع على انضمام قيد زائد إليه فقد كان هذا الشيء قبل انضمام هذا القيد الزائد إليه ممتنع الوقوع ، فحين حكمنا عليه بأنه كان أولى بالوقوع، فقد كان ممتنع الوقوع هذا خلف. وإن لم يتوقف على انضمام قيد إليه . فنقول : نسبة حصول تلك الأولوية إلى الوقتين أعني وقت حصول الأثر ، ووقت عدم الأثر على السوية . حاصلة في الوقتين على صورة واحدة ، وكيفية واحدة ، فاختصاص أحد ذينك الوقتين بالوقوع دون الوقت الثاني ، يكون رجحاناً لأحد طرفي الممكن المتساوي على الآخر لا لمرجح . وذلك محال . لأن هذه المسألة مفرعة على بيان أن القادر لا يصدر عنه الفعل إلا عند حصول الداعية المرجحة. وهذا أيضاً برهان قاطع في هذه المسألة .
The second proof: on demonstrating that when the preponderating motive occurs, the act becomes necessary of occurrence: that we say: When preponderance occurs on the side of existence, either it is impossible or not impossible. If it is impossible, then this is the desired result, because everything whose non-existence is impossible, its existence is necessary. And if it is not impossible, we say: Everything that is not impossible, it is not necessary that assuming its occurrence leads to an impossibility. Let us then suppose, when that preponderance occurs, that effect sometimes occurs and sometimes does not occur. So the time of occurrence is distinguished from the time of non-occurrence. Either it depends on the addition of an extra restriction to it for which it becomes more deserving of occurrence, or it does not depend. If its occurrence depends on the addition of an extra restriction to it, then this thing before the addition of this extra restriction to it was impossible of occurrence. So when we judged that it was more deserving of occurrence, then it was impossible of occurrence – this is a contradiction. And if it does not depend on the addition of a restriction to it, we say: The relation of obtaining that priority to the two times – meaning the time of obtaining the effect and the time of not obtaining the effect – is equal. It obtains in both times in one form and one manner. So the specification of one of those two times for occurrence rather than the other time would be a preponderance of one of the two sides of the equally possible over the other without a preponderator. And that is impossible. Because this issue is derived from the demonstration that the capable agent (al-qādir) does not produce the act except when the preponderating motive is present. And this is also a decisive proof in this matter.
Source pages p. 57

Commentary
This passage contains the second proof (al-ḥujja al-thāniya) that when the preponderating motive (al-dā'iya al-murjiha) exists, the act becomes necessary (wājib al-wuqūʿ). The proof examines the preponderance of the side of existence: either it is impossible or possible. If impossible, then the non-existence of the act is impossible, so its existence is necessary, which is the desired conclusion. If the preponderance is possible, then we suppose the effect sometimes occurs and sometimes not when that preponderance is present. Then we analyze whether the occurrence depends on an extra restriction (qayd zāʾid). If it does, then before that restriction the act was impossible—yet we judged it was more deserving of occurrence, a contradiction. If it does not depend on an extra restriction, then the priority (al-awlawiyya) is equal in relation to both times (the time of occurrence and the time of non-occurrence). Thus, specifying one time over the other would be a preponderance without a preponderator (lā li-murajjiḥ), which is impossible. Therefore, the act must be necessary. The proof rests on the principle that the capable agent (al-qādir) only acts when the preponderating motive is present.

Key Terms
داعية مرجحة
واجب الوقوع
ممتنع الوقوع
قيد زائد
أولوية
مرجح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that necessity only arises if preponderance is impossible, but the proof shows that even if preponderance is possible, it still leads to necessity due to the impossibility of arbitrary specification.
Correction: The proof demonstrates that both possibilities (preponderance being impossible or possible) lead to the necessity of the act. The second branch is eliminated by contradiction or impossibility, leaving only necessity.

Checkpoint
In the second proof, if the occurrence of the effect does not depend on an extra restriction, what problem arises?
A) The effect is impossible.
B) The effect is necessary.
C) The specification of one time would be a preponderance without a preponderator.
D) The motive is unnecessary.

Key Takeaway
The act is necessitated by the preponderating motive; any alternative leads to contradiction or an arbitrary preference without a cause, both impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you have a strong motive to do good (e.g., prayer), have you ever found yourself not acting? According to this proof, what must be missing from your motive? What extra restriction are you adding that leads to non-occurrence?
?
Consider a sin you commit repeatedly despite knowing it's wrong. What does this proof suggest about the state of your motive? Is the preponderance truly there? What would make your motive fully preponderating so that the act becomes necessary?
?
The proof eliminates arbitrary specification without reason. In your daily choices, do you ever act without a preponderating motive? How can you ensure your actions are grounded in sound reasons rather than arbitrary whims?

The Principle of Equality and the Preventive
Sanusi states the principle of equality and defines the preventive.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that when capability is equally related to act and omission, preponderance is impossible without a cause, and that a preponderating motive acts as a preventive for the opposite side.

Core Idea
Without a motive, preponderance is impossible.

The third proof begins with a foundational principle: when capability is equally related to both act and omission, no preponderance can occur. This leads to the concept of the preventive (al-māniʿ), which is crucial for the argument that follows.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the author states the principle of equality first, then defines the preventive.
The passage uses the term 'preponderance' (رجحان) frequently.
2
Understand the state of equality
When capability is equally related, neither the efficient cause (muqtaḍī) nor the preventive (māniʿ) exists. This is the starting point.
3
Identify the role of the motive
When a preponderating motive occurs for one side, it becomes a preventive for the opposite side. This is a key insight.
Preponderance of one contradictory acts as a preventive for the other.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة : إنا بينا أن عند استواء نسبة القدرة إلى الفعل وإلى الترك ، فإنه يمتنع رجحان أحد الجانبين على الآخر . إذا عرفت هذا . فنقول : إن عند استواء أحد الطرفين لم يوجد ، لا المقتضي للرجحان ، ولا المانع منه . وأما عند حصول الداعية المرجحة لأحد الجانبين ، فقد حصل المانع من رجحان الطرف الثاني . لأن رجحان أحد طرفي النقيض كالمانع من رجحان الطرف الثاني . وإذا عرفت هذا فنقول : إن عند حصول الاستواء ، لم يوجد المقتضي ولا المانع . ثم كان حصول الرجحان في هذا الوقت ممتنعاً ، فعند حصول رجحان أحد الطرفين لم يوجد المقتضي برجحان الطرف الثاني . وحصل المانع منه . لأنا بينا أن رجحان الطرف الأول مانع من رجحان الطرف الثاني. وإذا كان الشيء عند عدم المقتضى ممتنع الوقوع فبأن يصير عند عدم المقتضى وحصول المانع ممتنع الوقوع كان أولى . وإذا صار هذا الجانب ممتنع الوقوع، صار الجانب الثاني واجب الوقوع ضرورة أنه لا واسطة بين الوقوع وبين اللاوقوع . وهذا أيضاً برهان قاطع.
The third proof: We have demonstrated that when the capability (al-qudra) is equally related to the act and to the omission, then preponderance of one side over the other is impossible. If you understand this, then we say: When the two sides are equal, neither the efficient cause of preponderance (al-muqtaḍī) nor the preventive (al-māniʿ) exists. But when the preponderating motive for one of the two sides occurs, then the preventive from the preponderance of the second side has occurred. Because preponderance of one of the two contradictories is like a preventive from the preponderance of the second side. And if you understand this, we say: When equality exists, neither the efficient cause nor the preventive existed. Then the occurrence of preponderance at that time was impossible. So when preponderance of one side occurs, the efficient cause for the preponderance of the second side does not exist, and the preventive from it has occurred. Because we have shown that preponderance of the first side is a preventive from the preponderance of the second side. And when a thing is impossible of occurrence in the absence of the efficient cause, then it is even more appropriate that it becomes impossible of occurrence in the absence of the efficient cause and the presence of the preventive. And when this side becomes impossible of occurrence, the second side becomes necessary of occurrence necessarily, because there is no intermediate between occurrence and non-occurrence. And this is also a decisive proof.
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage presents the third proof (al-ḥujja al-thālitha) that the act becomes necessary when the preponderating motive exists. It starts from the established principle that when capability (al-qudra) is equally related to both act and omission, preponderance is impossible. Under equality, neither the efficient cause of preponderance (al-muqtaḍī) nor the preventive (al-māniʿ) exists. When the preponderating motive arises for one side, it provides a preventive against the other side, because preponderance of one contradictory itself acts as a preventive for the other. So now for the second side, the efficient cause is absent and the preventive is present. Since a thing is impossible in the absence of its efficient cause, it is even more impossible when a preventive is added. Therefore, the second side becomes impossible, and by the law of excluded middle (no intermediate between occurrence and non-occurrence), the first side becomes necessary. This is presented as a decisive proof (burhān qāṭiʿ).

Key Terms
قدرة
مقتضي
مانع
رجحان
داعية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that equality of capability alone can be broken arbitrarily without a cause.
Correction: The proof shows that without a cause (motive), preponderance is impossible, and the motive provides both the cause and a preventive for the opposite.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what happens when a preponderating motive occurs for one side?
A) The efficient cause for the opposite side is present.
B) The preventive for the opposite side has occurred.
C) Both sides become equal.
D) The opposite side becomes necessary.

Key Takeaway
Equality of capability makes preponderance impossible without a cause, and a preponderating motive for one side acts as a preventive for the other.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a recent decision where you were truly neutral—able to choose either option. Did you feel the need for a reason to tip the scale? What does that reveal about how you process choices? Now, confront this: what was the real motive that made you choose what you did? Was it pleasing Allah or something else?
?
You know that every sin is committed because some motive outweighed the command of Allah. What is the one recurring motive in your life that acts as a preventive against obedience? Name it clearly—not in general terms, but as a specific desire or habit. Once identified, what are you willing to sacrifice today to remove that preventive?
?
Consider the equality of capability between sin and obedience. Do you ever deceive yourself into thinking you can choose either without accountability? This lesson proves that a motive is always present. What motives have you been ignoring or justifying? Write down one today and ask yourself: does this motive truly deserve to tip the scale against Allah's command?

From Preventive to Necessity
Sanusi concludes the proof: preventive makes one side impossible, other side necessary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the absence of the efficient cause and the presence of the preventive make one side impossible and the other side necessary.

Core Idea
Absence of cause plus preventive makes impossible; opposite becomes necessary.

Building on the previous lesson, this part of the third proof uses the concept of the preventive to show that when one side becomes impossible, the other side must necessarily occur. The argument relies on the law of excluded middle: no intermediate between existence and non-existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice how the author uses conditional reasoning: if a thing is impossible without its cause, it is even more impossible with a preventive added.
The phrase 'أولى' indicates a logical priority.
2
Trace the logical steps
Under equality, no cause or preventive existed, so preponderance was impossible. When a motive gives preponderance to one side, that side's preponderance becomes a preventive for the other. For the other side, cause is absent and preventive is present, so it becomes impossible.
3
Understand the conclusion
When the second side is impossible, the first side must be necessary because there is no middle ground between occurrence and non-occurrence.
This is a decisive proof (burhān qāṭiʿ).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة : إنا بينا أن عند استواء نسبة القدرة إلى الفعل وإلى الترك ، فإنه يمتنع رجحان أحد الجانبين على الآخر . إذا عرفت هذا . فنقول : إن عند استواء أحد الطرفين لم يوجد ، لا المقتضي للرجحان ، ولا المانع منه . وأما عند حصول الداعية المرجحة لأحد الجانبين ، فقد حصل المانع من رجحان الطرف الثاني . لأن رجحان أحد طرفي النقيض كالمانع من رجحان الطرف الثاني . وإذا عرفت هذا فنقول : إن عند حصول الاستواء ، لم يوجد المقتضي ولا المانع . ثم كان حصول الرجحان في هذا الوقت ممتنعاً ، فعند حصول رجحان أحد الطرفين لم يوجد المقتضي برجحان الطرف الثاني . وحصل المانع منه . لأنا بينا أن رجحان الطرف الأول مانع من رجحان الطرف الثاني. وإذا كان الشيء عند عدم المقتضى ممتنع الوقوع فبأن يصير عند عدم المقتضى وحصول المانع ممتنع الوقوع كان أولى . وإذا صار هذا الجانب ممتنع الوقوع، صار الجانب الثاني واجب الوقوع ضرورة أنه لا واسطة بين الوقوع وبين اللاوقوع . وهذا أيضاً برهان قاطع.
The third proof: We have demonstrated that when the capability (al-qudra) is equally related to the act and to the omission, then preponderance of one side over the other is impossible. If you understand this, then we say: When the two sides are equal, neither the efficient cause of preponderance (al-muqtaḍī) nor the preventive (al-māniʿ) exists. But when the preponderating motive for one of the two sides occurs, then the preventive from the preponderance of the second side has occurred. Because preponderance of one of the two contradictories is like a preventive from the preponderance of the second side. And if you understand this, we say: When equality exists, neither the efficient cause nor the preventive existed. Then the occurrence of preponderance at that time was impossible. So when preponderance of one side occurs, the efficient cause for the preponderance of the second side does not exist, and the preventive from it has occurred. Because we have shown that preponderance of the first side is a preventive from the preponderance of the second side. And when a thing is impossible of occurrence in the absence of the efficient cause, then it is even more appropriate that it becomes impossible of occurrence in the absence of the efficient cause and the presence of the preventive. And when this side becomes impossible of occurrence, the second side becomes necessary of occurrence necessarily, because there is no intermediate between occurrence and non-occurrence. And this is also a decisive proof.

Commentary
This passage presents the third proof (al-ḥujja al-thālitha) that the act becomes necessary when the preponderating motive exists. It starts from the established principle that when capability (al-qudra) is equally related to both act and omission, preponderance is impossible. Under equality, neither the efficient cause of preponderance (al-muqtaḍī) nor the preventive (al-māniʿ) exists. This is presented as a decisive proof (burhān qāṭiʿ).

The Fourth Proof: Impossibility of Omission When Motive Is Present
Sanusi presents the fourth proof that omission is impossible when motive exists.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth proof's argument that when the motive to act is present, omission is impossible, and how it relies on the principle that every preference requires a cause.

Core Idea
A present motive makes omission impossible; any alternative leads to contradiction.

This lesson presents Sanusi's fourth proof, a precise argument in Ash'ari metaphysics about the relationship between motive and action. The proof uses a dichotomy and reduction to absurdity to show that if the motive to act exists, omission cannot occur. Understanding this argument clarifies the rational basis for divine action and human agency within the Ash'ari framework.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the structure: thesis, division, refutation of each case, conclusion.
The proof uses two possibilities: omission due to a motive or without a motive.
2
Identify the two cases and why each is impossible
The first case (omission due to a motive) fails because two motives conflict. The second (omission without a motive) fails because preference would occur without a cause.
The principle 'preference requires a cause' is key.
3
Understand the conclusion and its implication
Since both possibilities are false, omission is impossible when motive is present. This establishes a necessary connection between motive and action.
This does not imply compulsion but rational necessity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الرابعة: إن عند قيام الداعي إلى الفعل يمتنع حصول الترك. وذلك لأنه لو حصل الترك، لحصل إما للداعي، أو لا للداعي. والأول باطل، لأنه عند حصول الداعي إلى الفعل يمتنع حصول الداعي إلى الترك، وبتقدير أن يحصلا، يتعارضان فيتساقطان، ولم يبق الداعي إلى الفعل راجع الوقوع. وكلا منا فيما إذا بقيت تلك الداعية على صفة الرجحان، والثاني أيضاً باطل، لأن عند قيام الداعي إلى الفعل، لو حصل الترك، لا للداعي أصلاً، لكان قد حصل الرجحان من غير حصول الداعي. والكلام في هذه المسألة فرع على أن حصول الترجيح يتوقف على حصول الداعي، ولما ثبت فساد القسمين ثبت أن عند حصول الداعي إلى الفعل كان الترك محال الوقوع
The fourth proof: When the motive (al-dāʿī) to action exists, the occurrence of omission (al-tark) is impossible. This is because if omission occurred, it would occur either (a) due to a motive, or (b) not due to a motive. The first is false, because when the motive to action exists, a motive to omission cannot exist; and if they were to exist, they would conflict and cancel each other out, leaving no motive to action for occurrence. And it is inconceivable that the motive would remain preponderant in that case. The second is also false, because when the motive to action exists, if omission occurred without any motive at all, then preference would have occurred without a motive. This discussion is a branch of the principle that the occurrence of preference (tarjīḥ) depends on the occurrence of the motive. And since the falsity of both divisions is established, it is established that when the motive to action exists, omission is impossible of occurrence.
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth proof (al-ḥujja al-rābiʿa) in a kalām argument about the necessity of action when a motive to act is present. The author begins by stating the thesis: when the motive to act (dāʿī ilā al-fiʿl) is present, omission (tark) is impossible. The proof proceeds by dividing the possibilities: if omission were to occur, it would either be caused by a motive or not caused by any motive. The first case is false because if a motive to act exists, a motive to omit cannot simultaneously exist—if both existed, they would conflict and neutralize each other, and the original motive would no longer be preponderant. The second case is also false because if omission occurred without any motive, that would imply a preference (tarjīḥ) without a cause, which contradicts the established principle that preference requires a motive. Therefore, both possibilities are impossible, so when the motive to act is present, omission must be impossible. The argument relies on the foundational premise that every preference requires a cause (dāʿī), a core tenet of Ashʿarī occasionalism.

Key Terms
داعي
ترك
ترجيح
تساقط
رجحان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Omission can occur when a motive to act is present, either by another motive or without any motive.
Correction: The proof shows both possibilities are impossible, so omission cannot occur when motive exists.

Checkpoint
According to the fourth proof, what are the two possible ways omission could occur when a motive to act exists?
A) Omission occurs due to the same motive or due to no motive.
B) Omission occurs due to a different motive or due to no motive.
C) Omission occurs by chance or by external constraint.
D) Omission occurs through indecision or through ignorance.

Key Takeaway
When a motive to act exists, omission is impossible because both possible causes are rationally excluded.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you know a truth but fail to act on it, what is the hidden motive behind that omission? This argument forces you to admit that your inaction must have a cause—so what is it, and is it stronger than your motive to obey?
?
Have you ever excused your neglect of a religious duty by saying 'I just didn't feel like it'? Does this passage show that such an excuse is irrational, because 'not feeling like it' is still a motive—one that you must now account for before Allah?
?
If every preference requires a motive, then your daily choices reveal what you truly prefer. What do your omissions—prayers missed, charity withheld, anger unchecked—tell you about the motives that dominate your heart? Will you confront them, or continue to ignore the contradiction?

First Objection: The Agent Can Change the Motive
Opponents argue that action is not obligatory because the agent can change his motive.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first objection against the claim that motive necessitates action: that the agent can change the motive, which would be impossible if action were necessary.

Core Idea
If action were necessary, the agent couldn't alter the motive—but he can, so it's not necessary.

The discussion on whether motive makes action obligatory begins with an objection. Opponents argue that the presence of a motive does not compel action, because the agent retains the ability to change that motive. This lesson examines their first argument, which highlights the agent's capacity to modify his motivations.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage carefully, then the English translation. Note the structure of the argument.
The passage is short but contains two parts: the main claim and an example.
2
Identify the key claim
Find the claim that the agent can change his motive. Understand what this implies about the necessity of action.
3
Reflect on the example
Consider the example of conflicting motives and the choice among three expiations. How does this support the argument?
This analogy shows that agents can choose among motives.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واحتج من قال: إن عند حصول الداعي إلى الفعل لا يصير الفعل واجباً بوجوه: الحجة الأولى: إن الذي دعا الداعي إلى الفعل، قد يمكنه أن يغير تلك الداعية، ولو كان ذلك واقعاً على سبيل الوجوب، لما قدر عليه. وأيضاً: فقد تتقابل الدواعي. ومع ذلك فإنه يرجح أحدها مثل المخير في الكفارات الثلاثة. والحجة الثانية: إنا قد دللنا على أن الفعل بدون الداعي محال، فلو فلنا مع هذا: إن الفعل يصير واجب الوقوع عند حصول الداعي، فحينئذ لا يبقى فرق بين الموجب وبين المختار. وذلك معلوم البطلان. ولأنه لو لم يبق الفرق بين الموجب وبين المختار، وجب أن لا يستحق المختار على فعله المدح
And he who said that when the motive to action occurs, the action does not become obligatory argued with several points: The first argument: The one who calls the motive to action can change that motive; if the action occurred necessarily, he would not be able to do so. Also, motives can conflict. Yet he gives preponderance to one of them, like the one given a choice among the three expiations. The second argument: We have demonstrated that action without a motive is impossible. If we also say that the action becomes necessary of occurrence when the motive occurs, then there would be no difference between the necessitated (al-mūjib) and the volitional agent (al-mukhtār). That is known to be false. And if there were no difference between the necessitated and the volitional agent, then the volitional agent would not deserve praise for his action.
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage presents objections (iḥtijāj) raised by those who deny that the presence of a motive (dāʿī) makes the action obligatory (wājib). The author lists several arguments. The first argument (al-ḥujja al-ūlā) states that the agent who has the motive can change that motive; if the action were necessary, he would be unable to do so. Additionally, motives can conflict, and the agent can give preponderance to one over another, as in the case of a person given a choice among three expiations (kaffārāt). This shows that action is not necessitated. The second argument (al-ḥujja al-thāniya) builds on the established premise that action without a motive is impossible. The objector argues that if we then also hold that action becomes necessary once the motive is present, then there would be no distinction between a necessitated being (al-mūjib) and a volitional agent (al-mukhtār). This is patently false, because we know that a volitional agent is praised for his actions, whereas a necessitated being is not. The text cuts off here, but the argument implies that the agent's freedom and moral responsibility would be undermined if the motive necessitated action.

Key Terms
داعي
وجوب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that if a motive is present, the agent has no choice but to act.
Correction: The objection shows that the agent can still alter or override his motive, so action is not forced.

Checkpoint
What does the ability to change a motive demonstrate according to the first objection?
A) That motives are irrelevant.
B) That action is not obligatory.
C) That expiations are always chosen.
D) That motives always conflict.

Key Takeaway
The first objection argues that the agent's ability to change his motive disproves the necessity of action.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a time you felt a strong motive to do something wrong but, for Allah's sake, you changed your mind. What does that tell you about your responsibility? Will you remember that moment next time you say 'I couldn't help it'?
?
If action were forced by motive, you could blame your sins on your desires. This argument says you cannot. What 'motive' have you been using as an excuse? Starting today, what will you actively choose to change?
?
The example of choosing an expiation shows you have power to pick the best response. When you sin, do you rush to the easiest 'expiation'—or do you seek the one that pleases Allah most? What will you choose this week to purify your soul?

Second Objection: Motive Would Eliminate Distinction Between Necessitated and Volitional Agent
Opponents argue that if motive necessitated action, there would be no difference between a necessitated being and a volitional agent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second objection against the claim that motive necessitates action: that it would eliminate the distinction between a necessitated being and a volitional agent.

Core Idea
If motive necessitated action, free agents would be no different from determined ones.

The second objection against the necessity of action upon motive builds on the premise that action without a motive is impossible. Opponents argue that if we also hold that motive makes action necessary, then we cannot distinguish between a free agent and a determined one. This lesson examines that argument and its implications for moral responsibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and translation. Pay attention to the logical structure: premise, conditional, consequence.
The passage ends with the consequence that the volitional agent would not deserve praise.
2
Trace the logic
Identify the premise (action impossible without motive), the added claim (motive makes it necessary), and the consequence (no difference between necessitated and volitional).
Notice that the argument assumes that a necessitated being does not receive praise.
3
Consider the conclusion
Why is the consequence considered false? How does this undermine the original claim?
The falsity of the consequence shows that the added claim must be rejected.

The Fourth Proof: Motivation Necessitates Action
Sanusi presents the fourth proof that motivation necessitates action.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's fourth proof that when a genuine motivation for an action exists, the omission of that action is rationally impossible.

Core Idea
Genuine motivation makes omission impossible without contradiction.

This lesson introduces a crucial argument in the debate on causality and free will. Sanusi presents a proof that if a motivation for an action is present, it would be contradictory for the agent to omit the action. The argument is based on the principle that any preference or choice requires a motivating factor. Let's examine the reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Understand the claim
Read the passage and identify the conclusion: when motivation to an action exists, omission is impossible.
The claim is about rational impossibility, not physical compulsion.
2
Analyze the first possibility
Consider the first option: omission occurs due to a motivation. Why is this impossible? Because a motivation to omit would conflict with the existing motivation to act.
Think about what happens when two motivations conflict.
3
Analyze the second possibility
Consider the second option: omission occurs without any motivation. Why is this absurd? Because it would mean a preference (tarjih) happened without a cause.
This challenges the principle that every preference requires a motivator.
4
Grasp the underlying principle
Notice the principle: preference depends on motivation. This is key to the proof.
This principle is a foundation of kalam causation.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
Since both possibilities are invalid, the conclusion stands: omission is impossible when motivation is present.
Consider implications for human agency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الرابعة : إن عند قيام الداعي إلى الفعل يمتنع حصول الترك . وذلك لأنه لو حصل الترك ، لحصل إما للداعي ، أو لا للداعي والأول باطل ، لأنه عند حصول الداعي إلى الفعل يمتنع حصول الداعي إلى الترك ، وبتقدير أن يحصلا ، يتعارضان فيتساقطان ، ولم يبق الداعي إلى الفعل راجع الوقوع . وكلا منا فيما إذا بقيت تلك الداعية على صفة الرجحان ، والثاني أيضاً باطل ، لأن عند قيام الداعي إلى الفعل ، لو حصل الترك ، لا للداعي أصلاً ، لكان قد حصل الرجحان من غير حصول الداعي . والكلام في هذه المسألة فرع على أن حصول الترجيح يتوقف على حصول الداعي ، ولما ثبت فساد القسمين ثبت أن عند حصول الداعي إلى الفعل كان الترك محال الوقوع
The fourth proof: That when the motivation to the action exists, the occurrence of omission (tark) is impossible. That is because if omission occurred, it would occur either due to a motivation, or not due to a motivation. The first is false, because when the motivation to the action exists, the occurrence of motivation to omission is impossible; and if it were supposed that both occur, they would conflict and cancel each other out, and the motivation to the action would not remain in a state of preponderance. And likewise regarding us (?) if that motivation remains in a state of preponderance. The second is also false, because when the motivation to the action exists, if omission occurred not due to a motivation at all, then preponderance would have occurred without the existence of a motivation. And the discussion on this issue is subsidiary to the principle that the occurrence of preference depends on the occurrence of motivation. And since the corruption of both divisions is established, it is established that when the motivation to the action exists, omission is impossible in occurrence.
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth proof that when a motivation (da'i) for an action is present, the omission of that action is impossible. The argument proceeds by dividing the possible causes of omission: either it occurs due to a motivation, or not due to a motivation. The first option is false because if a motivation for omission existed, it would conflict with the existing motivation for action, leading to cancellation of both, leaving the original motivation ineffectual—but it remains preponderant. The second option is false because omission without any motivation would mean preference (tarjih) occurred without a motivating factor, which is impossible. The discussion depends on the principle that preference requires motivation. Since both options are invalid, the conclusion holds: when motivation for an action exists, omission cannot occur.

Key Terms
الداعي (al-da'i): motivation
الترك (al-tark): omission
الرجحان (al-rujhan): preponderance
الترجيح (al-tarjih): preference
المحال (al-muhal): impossible

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof denies free will and implies determinism.
Correction: The proof does not deny free will entirely; it argues that genuine motivation makes the action necessary in the sense that it would be contradictory to omit without a contrary motivation. This is a rational necessity, not a physical compulsion.

Checkpoint
According to the fourth proof, when motivation to an action exists, omission is impossible because:
A) Omission would require a contrary motivation, which is impossible or leads to conflict.
B) The agent is compelled by external force.
C) Motivation always leads to action by definition.
D) The action is predetermined by divine decree.

Key Takeaway
The fourth proof establishes that when a genuine motivation for an action exists, the omission of that action is rationally impossible because it would either lead to a conflict of motivations or to an uncaused preference.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a good deed you know you should do but haven't done. According to this proof, if you had genuine, preponderant motivation, omission would be impossible. Does your lack of action reveal a lack of genuine motivation? What would it take for that motivation to truly outweigh competing desires?
?
The proof argues that every preference requires a motivator. Examine a recent choice you made that you regret. Was there a real motivator behind that choice, or did you act without genuine reason? How can you ensure your motivations align with what you truly value?
?
If you believe in the truth of this proof, then your actions are a direct reflection of your motivations. Are you content with what your actions say about your inner state? What specific change in your motivation would lead to a different action starting today?

First Objection: Motivation Can Be Changed and Conflicting Motivations
The opponent objects that the agent can change the motivation and that motivations can conflict.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first objection against the claim that motivation necessarily compels action, including the arguments from changeability and conflict of motivations.

Core Idea
Motivation does not necessitate action because agents can change motivations and resolve conflicts.

The opponent of the necessitarian view raises a critical objection: if a motivation truly compelled an action, the agent would have no power to change it. Yet we experience changing our motivations and weighing conflicting options. This passage presents that challenge with two concise arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Let the opponent's logic come through before interpreting.
Notice the two separate arguments introduced by 'وأيضاً'.
2
Identify the first argument: changeability
The opponent argues that the one who has a motivation can change it. If the action were necessary (wajib) due to the motivation, he would not be able to change it. That implies the action is not obligatory.
The term 'الواجب' here means necessary/compelled, not moral obligation.
3
Identify the second argument: conflict of motivations
The opponent notes that motivations can conflict. Yet the agent can still prefer one over another, like choosing among three expiations. This shows motivation does not force a single outcome.
The example of 'الكفارات الثلاثة' refers to three types of expiation for a broken oath (feeding, clothing, freeing a slave).
4
Reflect on the overall objection
Consider how these two points challenge the idea that motivation necessarily causes action. If the agent can change or choose between motivations, then the action is not deterministically compelled.
This objection is central to debates about free will and causal determinism in qadar.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واحتج من قال : إن عند حصول الداعي إلى الفعل لا يصير الفعل واجباً بوجوه : الحجة الأولى : إن الذي دعا الداعي إلى الفعل ، قد يمكنه أن يغير تلك الداعية ، ولو كان ذلك واقعاً على سبيل الوجوب ، لما قدر عليه . وأيضاً : فقد تتقابل الدواعي . ومع ذلك فإنه يرجح أحدها مثل المخير في الكفارات . الثلاثة
And he argued who said: That when the motivation to the action occurs, the action does not become obligatory (wajib) by ways: The first proof: That the one who is motivated (?) to the action, he is able to change that motivation; and if that (i.e., the action) were occurring in the manner of obligation, he would not be able to do so. And also: Motivations may conflict. Nevertheless, he gives preponderance to one of them, like the one who has a choice among the three expiations.
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage presents the first objection from those who claim that the presence of motivation does not make the action obligatory (wajib). The opponent gives two arguments: (1) The agent who has a motivation can change that motivation; if the action were necessary (obligatory) given the motivation, he would not be able to change it. (2) Motivations can conflict (as in the case of someone choosing among three types of expiation for a broken oath), yet the agent is able to prefer one over the others, implying that motivation does not determine the action necessarily.

Key Terms
الداعي (al-da'i): motivation
الواجب (al-wajib): obligatory/necessary
المخير (al-mukhayyar): one who has a choice
الكفارات (al-kaffarat): expiations

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection confuses logical necessity with moral obligation; the discussion is about causal necessitation, not ethical duty.
Correction: The opponent uses 'wajib' to mean necessary/compelled, not morally obligatory. The Ash'ari position on qadar is about causal determinism, not ethical requirements.

Checkpoint
The first objection argues that if motivation necessitated action:
A) The agent would not be able to change the motivation.
B) The agent would be compelled.
C) Conflicting motivations would still lead to action.
D) The action would be eternal.

Key Takeaway
The first objection against necessitarian causation is that agents can change motivations and resolve conflicts, implying that motivation does not compel action.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a time when you felt a strong desire to do something you knew was wrong, but you changed your mind. What does that ability to change your motivation say about whether your desire was a compulsion? How did you actually change it?
?
When you face conflicting desires—like wanting to sleep vs. wanting to pray Fajr—you choose one. On what basis do you make that choice? Are you aware that your choice reflects your real priorities, not just which motivation was 'stronger'?
?
If every motivation forced you to act, you would have no real responsibility for your choices. But you know you are accountable. When have you used the excuse 'I couldn't help it' to avoid taking responsibility? How does this passage challenge that excuse?

Second Objection: Necessitation Eliminates Agent Distinction
Opponent argues necessity eliminates distinction between free and compelled agent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second objection against the Ash'ari view that motivation necessitates action, and why the opponent argues it destroys the distinction between free and compelled agents.

Core Idea
If action is necessitated by motivation, then free will and moral responsibility are illusions.

The opponent now presents a second argument: if we accept that an action becomes necessary upon the arrival of motivation, then there would be no difference between a free agent and a compelled one. This would undermine the very foundations of moral responsibility—praise, blame, commands, and prohibitions. Let's examine this objection closely.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to grasp the overall argument.
The corrected word 'قلنا' replaces 'فلنا'.
2
Identify the core conditional claim
Notice the conditional structure: 'If we say the action becomes necessary when motivation occurs, then there is no difference between the compelled and the free agent.'
This is the heart of the objection.
3
Analyze the moral consequences
The opponent argues that if no distinction exists, then praise, blame, commands, and prohibitions become meaningless for free agents. Consider why these would be invalid for a compelled agent like fire.
These consequences are meant to show the premise is false.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والحجة الثانية : إنا قد دللنا على أن الفعل بدون الداعي محال ، فلو فلنا مع هذا : إن الفعل يصير واجب الوقوع عند حصول الداعي ، فحينئذ لا يبقى فرق بين الموجب وبين المختار . وذلك معلوم البطلان . ولأنه لو لم يبق الفرق بين الموجب وبين المختار ، وجب أن لا يستحق المختار على فعله المدح والذم والترغيب والترهيب . وأن لا يحسن أمره ولا نهيه ، كما لا يحسن كل ذلك في حق الموجب. ومعلوم أن كل ذلك باطل
And the second proof: We have already shown that the action without motivation is impossible. So if we say: The action becomes necessary in occurrence when the motivation occurs, then there remains no difference between the necessitated (al-mujib) and the free agent (al-mukhtar). And that is known to be false. And because if there remains no difference between the necessitated and the free agent, then the free agent must not deserve for his action praise, blame, enticement, deterrence; and it would not be good to command him or forbid him, just as none of that is good in the case of the necessitated. And it is known that all that is false.
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage presents the second objection: we have already proven that an action without motivation is impossible. If we then say that when motivation occurs the action becomes necessary (wajib al-wuqu'), then there would be no difference between a 'necessitated' (mujib: a compelled being like inanimate nature) and a 'free agent' (mukhtar). This is known to be false because, if there were no difference, the free agent would not deserve praise or blame, nor would commands or prohibitions be appropriate for him, unlike the necessitated (e.g., fire burning). Since these are clearly valid, the premise must be false.

Key Terms
الموجب
المختار
المدح
الذم
الترغيب
الترهيب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the second objection, if motivation necessitates action, what would be lost?
A) The distinction between compelled and free agents.
B) The existence of motivation.
C) The possibility of inaction.
D) The justice of God.

Key Takeaway
The opponent argues that if motivation necessitates action, the distinction between a free agent and a compelled agent collapses, undermining moral responsibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you follow your strongest desire, do you ever feel as though you had no choice? How does this passage challenge that feeling, and what will you do the next time that feeling arises?
?
Consider a time you blamed yourself for a sin. Did you truly believe you could have done otherwise? If not, then according to this argument, would that blame be unjust? What does that reveal about your view of your own responsibility?
?
This passage argues that if motivation necessitates action, then commands and prohibitions would be pointless. Think of a command Allah has given you that you find difficult. Do you truly believe you have the freedom to obey or disobey despite your internal motivations? How will you exercise that freedom today?

Third Objection: Intuitive Distinction Between Compelled and Voluntary
The opponent objects with an intuitive distinction argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third common objection against the necessitarian view of motivation: that we directly experience a distinction between compulsion and free choice.

Core Idea
Our immediate awareness of free choice refutes the idea that motivation forces action.

This lesson presents one of the most intuitive objections to the claim that motivation necessitates action. The opponent argues that we all, by necessary knowledge, recognize a clear difference between being compelled to act and acting freely. If motivation were a forced cause, this experienced distinction would vanish. As you read the passage, notice how the objection relies on immediate human experience.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection passage
Read the Arabic sentence and its translation carefully. Note the key terms: mulja' (compelled) and mukhtar (free).
2
Identify the argument's structure
The objection has two parts: (1) a claim of direct knowledge of a distinction, (2) a conditional: if motivation necessitated action, the distinction would disappear.
Look for the word 'law' (if).
3
Reflect on the intuitive appeal
Consider why this objection feels powerful. It appeals to everyday experience. Why might the necessitarian respond that the distinction is compatible with motivation as a 'sufficient reason'?
This prepares for the missing answer.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والحجة الثالثة : إنا نعلم بالضرورة حصول التفرقة بين ما إذا كان الإنسان ملجأ إلى الفعل ، وبين ما يكون مختاراً فيه . ولو كان الداعي موجباً للفعل لما بقي هذا الفرق . والله أعلم
And the third proof: We know by necessity the existence of a distinction between when a man is compelled (mulja') to an action and when he is free (mukhtar) in it. And if motivation necessitated the action, this difference would not remain. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage presents the third objection: we know by necessary knowledge (al-ilm al-daruri) that there is a fundamental distinction between being compelled (mulja') to an action and being free (mukhtar) in it. If the presence of motivation made the action necessary (i.e., if it were a necessitating cause), then this intuitive distinction would disappear, because even when we act voluntarily, it would be like being compelled. Since we clearly experience this difference, the claim that motivation necessitates action is false. The objector concludes with 'And God knows best.'

الفرق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This objection proves that motivation is completely irrelevant to action.
Correction: The objection only argues against necessitation, not against motivation being a cause that influences action without removing free choice.

Checkpoint
According to the third objection, what does our necessary knowledge tell us?
A) That motivation always leads to action.
B) That there is a difference between being compelled and being free.
C) That God compels all actions.
D) That motivation can be changed.

Key Takeaway
The intuitive distinction between compulsion and free choice is presented as evidence that motivation does not force action.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
You know by necessity that you are not coerced in your choices. Then why do you sometimes act as if your lower self has no will of its own? Repent of blaming your nafs for what you freely chose, and take ownership of your deeds tonight.

Answering the First Objection: Change of Motivation Does Not Undermine Necessity
Sanusi answers the first objection, distinguishing changeability from conditional necessity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the possibility of changing motivation does not contradict the necessity of action when motivation is present, and why the subsidiary argument cannot invalidate the principle.

Core Idea
Changeable motivation still necessitates action when it actually exists.

Sanusi now faces the first objection head-on. The opponent claims that if the agent can change the motivation, then the action is not necessary. Sanusi clarifies that the necessity is conditional on the motivation being present, not on its immutability. He then exposes a second flaw: the opponent's argument about equal motivations has already been refuted and cannot be used to undermine the original principle.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation once to get the flow. Note how Sanusi distinguishes between the possibility of change and the necessity when the motivation is present.
Pay attention to the terms 'al-da'i' (motivation) and 'al-mu'arid' (opposition).
2
Understand the first point: change does not negate necessity
The opponent says the agent can change the motivation. Sanusi replies that this does not affect his claim: when the motivation exists (without restrictions or opposition), the action necessarily follows. The possibility of changing it beforehand is irrelevant.
3
Analyze the second point: equal motivations already refuted
The opponent's second argument (equal motivations leading to action without preference) is a return to the previously refuted idea that action can occur without any motivation. Sanusi dismisses this as already invalid.
This shows the importance of not re-arguing settled matters.
4
Reflect on the subsidiary-principle relationship
Sanusi notes that the current issue (effect of motivation) is subsidiary to the previous issue (action without motivation). Arguing from the subsidiary to corrupt the principle is logically invalid.
This highlights a key logical principle in kalam: branches cannot overturn roots.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب عن الحجة الأولى : أن نقول : إن قولكم : إنه لا يمكنه أن يغير تلك الداعية : كلام ضعيف ، لأن الذي ادعيناه أن عند بقاء تلك الداعية خالية من القيود وعن المعارض، فإنه يجب صدور الفعل . وأنتم ذكرتم أن تلك الداعية قابلة للتغير. وهذا لا يقدح فيها ذكرناه البتة ، لأن قولنا : إن تلك الداعية يمكن تغيرها ، لا يقدح في قولنا : إنها عند حصولها يجب ترتب الأثر عليها ، وأما قوله : إن عند تساوي الدواعي ، فقد يفعل الفاعل الفعل ، فهذا عود إلى المسألة المتقدمة ) وهي أن صدور الفعل عن القادر بدون الداعي جائز ، وقد سبق إبطاله . وأيضاً : فهذه المسألة . متفرعة على تلك المسألة . والكلام في الفرع بحيث يوجب فساد الأصل : كلام باطل
And the answer to the first proof: that we say: Your saying: 'He cannot change that motivation' is a weak statement, because what we claim is that when that motivation remains free from restrictions and from opposition, then the action must occur. And you have mentioned that that motivation is subject to change. This does not impugn what we mentioned at all, because our saying: 'That motivation can be changed' does not impugn our saying: 'When it occurs, the effect must follow from it.' As for his saying: 'When motivations are equal, the agent may perform the action,' this is a return to the previous issue—namely, that the occurrence of the action from the capable agent without motivation is permissible, and it has already been refuted. And also, this issue is subsidiary to that issue. And discussing in the subsidiary in a way that necessitates the corruption of the principle is an invalid discussion.
Source pages p. 60

Commentary
This passage provides the answer to the first objection. The response clarifies that the claim made by the opponent (that the agent can change the motivation) is weak. The author's own claim is that when the motivation exists free from restrictions and without opposition, the action must occur. The fact that motivation is subject to change does not contradict that when it does exist, it necessitates the action. As for the second part about conflicting motivations and the agent preferring one, this returns to a previous issue (namely, that action can occur from a capable agent without motivation), which has already been refuted. Moreover, this current issue (the effect of motivation) is subsidiary to that previous one (the possibility of action without motivation), so arguing from the subsidiary to invalidate the principle is a flawed argument.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If motivation can change, then action is never necessary.
Correction: Necessity is conditional on the motivation being present and free from opposition. The fact that it could change beforehand does not negate its necessitating effect when it is present.

Checkpoint

A) Is correct, so the action is not necessary.

Key Takeaway
The first objection fails because the necessity of action is conditional on the presence of motivation, and the opponent's subsidiary argument cannot overturn the established principle.
