Two Differences Between Free Agent and Compelled Agent
Sanusi enumerates two differences between free agent and compelled agent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two key differences between a free agent and a compelled agent in Islamic theology, and why awareness and variability preserve free agency despite the necessity of motivation.

Core Idea
A free agent knows he is the source of action; a compelled agent does not, and his nature is fixed.

Sanusi now addresses a critical objection: if a free agent's action is necessitated by a decisive motivation, how is he different from a compelled agent? The answer lies in two specific distinctions: awareness and variability. This lesson unpacks both differences, showing why the free agent remains genuinely free despite the necessity of his motivation.

Guided Path
1
Read the answer passage carefully
Read the Arabic and English translation to grasp the two differences enumerated by Sanusi.
Notice the structure: 'two aspects: the first... the second...'
2
Analyze the first difference: awareness
Identify that the free agent knows he is the source of the effect, while the compelled agent does not.
This awareness is key to moral responsibility.
3
Analyze the second difference: variability
The compelled agent has one fixed nature; the free agent can have varying motivations.
This shows that free agency is not negated by the necessity of motivation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب عن الحجة الثانية : أن نقول : الفرق بين القادر وبين الموجب حاصل من وجهين : الأول : إن القادر حال حصول الداعية الجازمة في حقه يجب صدور ذلك الأثر عنه ، مع كونه عالماً بكونه مصدراً لذلك الأثر ، والموجب ليس كذلك . والفرق الثاني : إن الموجب بالطبع موصوف بصفة واحدة موجبة لا أثراً واحداً ولا تتغير تلك الصفة وتلك الطبيعة
And the answer to the second proof: That we say: The difference between the capable (al-qadir) and the necessitated (al-mujib) is established from two aspects: The first: That the capable agent, at the occurrence of the decisive motivation (al-da'iyya al-jazima) in his case, the occurrence of that effect from him is necessary, while he is knowing (alim) that he is the source (masdar) of that effect. But the necessitated is not so. The second difference: That the necessitated by nature is described with a single necessitating attribute (sifa wajiba), not a single effect (?), and that attribute and that nature do not change.
Source pages p. 60

Commentary
This passage provides the answer to the second objection by establishing two differences between the free agent (al-qadir) and the compelled agent (al-mujib). First difference: When a decisive motivation occurs for the free agent, the action necessarily proceeds from him, but he is conscious that he is the source of that effect—the compelled agent (e.g., fire) does not have this awareness. Second difference: The compelled agent (by nature) is described by a single necessitating quality that produces only one effect (e.g., fire only burns), and that quality and nature do not change. The free agent, by contrast, can have varying motivations and the ability to choose. Thus, motivation's necessitation does not eliminate the fundamental distinction, because awareness and variability preserve free agency.

Key Terms

الموجب (al-mujib)

مصدر (masdar)
عالماً (aliman)
بالصفة الموجبة (al-sifa al-mujiba)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The only difference between a free agent and a compelled agent is whether the action is necessitated.
Correction: The key difference is that the free agent is aware of being the source of the action, and his nature is not fixed like the compelled agent's.

Checkpoint

B) The free agent is aware he is the source of the action.
C) The compelled agent has multiple attributes.
D) The free agent cannot change his motivation.

Key Takeaway
The free agent differs from the compelled agent in two ways: awareness of being the source, and variability of nature.

The Two Types of Motivations and Their Proofs
The author divides motivations into two types and provides proofs for each.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two types of motivations that occur in the heart and the rational proofs for each, including the argument against infinite regress.

Core Idea
Motivations are either from the servant's act or directly from God's creation.

Motivations arise in our hearts, but where do they come from? Are they entirely our own doing, or do they originate from God? In this passage, the author lays out a clear division: some motivations are brought about by our own effort, while others are initially created by God. He then provides a proof for each type, using an argument that prevents an infinite regress. This lesson will walk you through these two categories and their rational foundations.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the English translation. Notice the structure: division, then proof for first type, then proof for second type.
Pay attention to the key terms: داعية (motivation) and تسلسل (infinite regress).
2
Identify the two types of motivations
The passage states that motivations are of two kinds: one that occurs by the act of the servant, and one that occurs by the creation of God. Underline these two categories in the text.
3
Understand the proof for the first type
The proof is experiential: we can change our motivations. For example, you can remove a desire from your heart through effort. This shows that some motivations are under our control.
4
Understand the proof for the second type
The proof uses logic: if every motivation to change came from us, we would need an infinite chain of motivations. This is impossible, so there must be a first motivation created by God.
The concept of tasalsul (infinite regress) is key here.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
The passage concludes that the chain of motivations ascends to a necessary motivation from God, from which all subsequent motivations branch necessarily. Think about how this reconciles human agency with divine creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التقسيم الأول: إن الدواعي التي تقع في القلوب على قسمين: منها ما يحصل في القلب بإيقاع العبد، ومنها: ما يحصل فيه ابتداء بتخليق الله تعالى. والدليل على صحة القول بالقسم الأول: إنا قد نقدر على تغيير الدواعي والبواعث، فقد يكون الواحد منا راغباً في شيء من الأشياء، وفي عمل من الأعمال، ثم إنه يسعى ويجتهد ويزيل عن قلبه تلك الرغبة، وذلك الميل. وهذا أمر وجداني يجده كل أحد من نفسه. وأما الدليل على صحة القول بالقسم الثاني: فهو إن قدرتنا على تغيير الداعية الحاصلة في القلب، لا يمكن أن تسبق داعية أخرى، فلو كانت تلك الداعية أيضاً منا، افتقرنا في تغيير تلك الداعية إلى داعية أخرى. ولزم التسلسل وهو محال. فيثبت: أن الأحوال الحادثة في القلوب ترتقي إلى داعية ضرورية حاصلة بتخليق الله تعالى ويتفرع عليها داعية ثانية، ويتفرع على تلك الداعية الثانية دواعي بالغة ما بلغت، ويكون تعلق كل داعية بما قبلها تعلقاً واقعاً على سبيل الوجوب واللزوم. وهذه كلمات من وفقه الله تعالى للتأمل فيها، وأزال عن قلبه غشاوة الشبه، واعتبر حال نفسه اعتباراً صحيحاً، وأن الأمر كما ذكرناه.
The first division: Verily, the motivations that occur in the hearts are of two kinds: among them is what occurs in the heart by the act of the servant, and among them is what occurs in it initially by the creation of Allah the Exalted. The proof for the validity of the first statement: we are indeed able to change motivations and incentives. For one of us may be desirous of something among things, or an action among actions, then he strives and exerts himself and removes from his heart that desire and that inclination. And this is an experiential matter that every person finds in himself. As for the proof for the validity of the second statement: it is that our ability to change the motivation occurring in the heart cannot be preceded by another motivation. For if that motivation were also from us, we would need another motivation to change that motivation. And an infinite regress would follow, which is impossible. So it is established that the occurring states in the hearts ascend to a necessary motivation produced by the creation of Allah the Exalted, and a second motivation branches from it, and from that second motivation branch motivations reaching however far they reach, and the connection of each motivation to what precedes it is a connection occurring in the manner of necessity and inherence. And these are words for whom Allah has guided to reflect upon them, and removed from his heart the veil of doubt, and considered his own state correctly, and that the matter is as we have mentioned.
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This passage presents the first division of motivations (al-dawa'i) that occur in the heart. The author states that motivations are of two types: (1) those that occur by the act of the servant (bi-iqa' al-'abd), meaning the servant actively brings them about, and (2) those that occur initially by the direct creation of Allah (takhliqu Allah ta'ala). He then provides a proof for each type. For the first type, he argues that we are able to change our motivations and incentives. For example, a person may desire something, then strive and remove that desire from his heart. This is an experiential fact that everyone finds in themselves. For the second type, he argues that our ability to change a motivation cannot be preceded by another motivation (i.e., the act of changing a motivation itself requires a motivation). If that changing motivation were also from us, we would need yet another motivation to bring it about, leading to an infinite regress (tasalsul), which is impossible. Therefore, the chain of motivations must ascend to a necessary motivation created by Allah from which subsequent motivations branch necessarily. The passage concludes by encouraging reflection on this matter.

Key Terms
دواعي
بواعث
تسلسل
تخليق الله

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All motivations are directly from God with no human role.
Correction: The author affirms that some motivations can be changed by the servant's act, but the chain ultimately originates from God.

Checkpoint

A) Because we can change our motivations experientially.
B) Because otherwise there would be an infinite regress of motivations.
C) Because all motivations are directly from God.
D) Because the servant has no power over motivations.

Key Takeaway
Motivations in the heart are either from the servant's act or from God's direct creation, but the chain necessarily begins with a motivation from God to avoid infinite regress.

Universal and Particular Motivations
The author explains universal vs. particular motivations and the need for specification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between universal and particular motivations and why a universal motivation alone cannot produce a particular action without a specifying factor.

Core Idea
A universal motivation equally relates to many particulars; only a specifying factor can tip the balance.

The author now moves from the general discussion of causality to a more precise classification of motivations. He distinguishes between universal and particular motivations, and explains a crucial principle: a universal motivation, by itself, cannot result in a specific action unless a particular motivation joins it or a specifying factor is present. This logical argument is foundational for understanding how divine and human actions are determined.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the English translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through.
Notice the examples he gives—they are intuitive.
2
Identify the two types of motivations
The passage defines universal and particular motivations. Find the examples: visiting a friend with multiple roads vs. moving a finger precisely.
Universal motivation is general; particular is specific to one action.
3
Grasp the key principle
The author states: a universal motivation alone cannot be the source of a particular action. Why? Because if many particulars are possible, the universal relates equally to all, so an additional specifying factor is needed to choose one.
This avoids the absurdity of preponderance without a preponderator.
4
Analyze the two cases
First case: the subject is receptive to many particulars—then a specifying factor is required. Second case: the subject can only accept one particular—then the universal motivation plus knowledge produce the particular motivation.
The second case is simpler; the first is the core argument.
5
Reflect on the implications
Why does this matter? It shows that actions are not arbitrary; they require sufficient reason. In theology, this principle helps explain divine choice among possibilities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التقسيم الثاني للدواعي: اعلم أن الداعي إلى الفعل قد يكون كلياً، وقد يكون جزئياً. مثال الداعي الكلي: أن يريد الرجل أن يذهب إلى زيارة صديق له، وكان من داره إلى دار صديقه طرق مختلفة. فإرادة الذهاب إلى دار الصديق إرادة كلية تندرج فيها جزئيات كثيرة، أعني الذهاب إليه من هذا الطريق، ومن ذلك الطريق، ومن الطريق الثالث. ومثال الداعية الجزئية: ما إذا أراد الإنسان أن يحرك إصبعه في هذه اللحظة اللطيفة من هذا الحد المعين، إلى ذلك الحد المعين. إذا عرفت هذا. فنقول: أما الداعية الكلية فإنها لا تصير مصدراً للفعل الجزئي إلا عند انضمام الداعية الجزئية إليها. وتقريره: أن عند حصول الداعية الكلية، فالمحل إما أن يكون قابلاً لأنواع كثيرة داخلة تحت ذلك الكلي. وإما أن لا يكون كذلك. فإن كان الأول كانت نسبة تلك الداعية الكلية إلى كل واحد من تلك الجزئيات على السوية. ومتى كان الأمر كذلك امتنع رجحان بعضهما على بعض، إلا لمخصص زائد، وإلا لزم ترجح أحد طرفي الممكن على الآخر، لا المرجيح وهو محال. وأما القسم الثاني: وهو أن تحصل الداعية الكلية. إلا أن المحل لا يقبل إلا نوعاً واحداً من الأنواع الداخلة تحت ذلك الجنس. فمثاله: ما إذا أراد الذهاب إلى لقاء صديقه ولا يمكنه الذهاب إليه إلا في طريق معين. فنقول ههنا: الداعية الكلية وهي إرادة الذهاب إلى لقاء ذلك الصديق مع العلم بأنه لا يمكن الذهاب إليه، إلا ماراً بذلك الطريق يوجبان إرادة جزئية. وهي إرادة
The second division of motivations: Know that the motivation to an action may be universal and may be particular. An example of the universal motivation: a man wants to go visit a friend, and there are different roads from his house to his friend's house. So the will to go to the friend's house is a universal will under which many particulars fall, I mean going to him by this road, that road, and the third road. An example of the particular motivation: when a person wants to move his finger in this precise moment from this determined limit to that determined limit. If you know this, we say: As for the universal motivation, it does not become a source for the particular action except when the particular motivation joins it. And its explanation: that when the universal motivation occurs, the subject is either receptive to many types falling under that universal, or it is not so. If it is the first, the relation of that universal motivation to each one of those particulars is equal. And when the matter is such, the preponderance of one over another is impossible unless there is an additional specifying factor, otherwise the preponderance of one of the two sides of the possible over the other without a preponderator would follow, which is impossible. As for the second case: that the universal motivation occurs, but the subject does not accept except one type of the types falling under that genus. Its example: when he wants to go meet his friend and he cannot go to him except by a specific road. We say here: the universal motivation, which is the will to go meet that friend, together with the knowledge that it is not possible to go to him except by passing that road, necessitate a particular motivation, which is the will
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This passage introduces the second division of motivations into universal (kulli) and particular (juz'i). It first gives examples: a universal motivation is the will to visit a friend when multiple roads are available; the will to go is universal because it encompasses many particular ways. A particular motivation is, e.g., the will to move one's finger at a precise moment from point A to point B. The author then explains that a universal motivation alone cannot produce a particular action unless a particular motivation joins it. He analyzes two scenarios: (1) When the subject is receptive to many particulars under the universal, the universal motivation relates equally to each particular, so without an additional specifying factor (mukhasṣiṣ), one particular cannot be chosen over another (otherwise it would be preponderance without a preponderator, which is impossible). (2) When the subject can only accept one particular (e.g., only one road exists), then the universal motivation together with the knowledge of that necessity produces the particular motivation. The passage presents the core kalam argument about the need for a specifying cause when multiple possibilities exist. It is cut off at the end, but the essential idea is clear.

Key Terms
داعية كلية
داعية جزئية
مخصص
ترجيح بلا مرجح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think a universal motivation directly produces a particular action without any additional factor.
Correction: The author shows that unless only one option is possible, a universal motivation equally relates to all particulars, so an additional specifying factor is necessary to avoid preponderance without a preponderator.

Checkpoint
When a universal motivation relates equally to many particulars, what is needed for one particular to occur?
A) A stronger universal motivation.
B) An additional specifying factor.
C) The absence of alternatives.
D) The subject's choice alone.

Key Takeaway
A universal motivation alone cannot determine a particular action; a specifying factor or a particular motivation is necessary.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have a universal intention to become closer to Allah, but many specific acts compete for your time. What is the one specifying factor you will consciously choose today to turn that general intention into a particular action? Write it down and commit to it.
?
When you make a decision, do you ever pause to ask: 'What is the real motivation behind this particular choice?' How does this passage reveal that even your small decisions are not independent but require a specifying factor from your Lord? Will you invite Him into your next decision?
?
If every particular action requires a specifying factor, and you know that Allah is the ultimate Creator of all motivations, does that change how you view your ability to act? Will you thank Him for the specific motivations He gives you, or do you take them for granted?

Universal Will Requires Particular Wills
The author proves that universal will is not a direct cause but requires particular wills.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a universal intention cannot directly cause particular actions without specific motives.

Core Idea
A general desire needs specific steps to become action.

In this passage, the author presents a logical proof that a universal motive (like intending to visit a friend) does not directly produce the specific actions required. Instead, it necessitates particular wills for each step. This clarifies the relationship between general and particular causality in human actions, which has implications for understanding divine will as well.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the logical structure: premise, example, deduction.
2
Identify the key terms
Find the Arabic terms for universal will, particular will, universal motive, particular motive, proximate cause.
3
Follow the proof
The author uses an example of going to someone via a specific path. Trace how the universal will to go necessitates the particular will to take that path.
4
Understand the conclusion
The conclusion states that the universal motive is not a proximate cause; particular motives are principles. Grasp why this matters.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الذهاب إليه في ذلك الطريق المعين. والدليل عليه: أنه لما أراد الذهاب إليه، وعلم أنه لا يمكن الذهاب إليه إلا بذلك الطريق فحينئذ نعلم أنه لا يمكنه تحصيل المطلوب إلا بهذا الطريق المعين. وما كان من لوازم المطلوب كان مطلوباً، فصارت تلك الإرادة الكلية موجبة لهذه الإرادة الجزئية فيثبت بمجموع ما ذكرنا أن الداعية الكلية لا تكون سبباً قريباً الحصول الأفعال الجزئية بل لا بد من دواعي جزئية تكون مبادىء للأفعال الجزئية.
The going to him along that specific path. The proof for it: that when he intended to go to him, and knew that he cannot go to him except by that path, then we know that he cannot attain the sought objective except by this specific path. And whatever is among the necessary concomitants of the sought objective is sought. So that universal will becomes necessitating for this particular will. So it is established by the sum of what we have mentioned that the universal motive is not a proximate cause for the occurrence of particular actions, but rather particular motives are necessary as principles for the particular actions.
Source pages p. 64

Commentary
This passage is a proof. The author begins with an example: a person wants to go to someone and knows that the only way is a specific path. From this, he argues that the universal will to go necessitates a particular will to take that path, because whatever is required to achieve the goal is itself desired. Therefore, the universal motive (al-da'iya al-kulliyya) is not a proximate cause (sabab qarib) of particular actions; instead, particular motives (dawa'i juz'iyya) are needed as principles (mabadi') for those actions. The conclusion guards against the misconception that a general intention suffices to directly produce complex actions.

Key Terms

الإرادة الجزئية
الداعية الكلية
الداعية الجزئية

Misconception: A universal intention (e.g., to visit a friend) is sufficient to directly produce the actions involved.
Correction: The author demonstrates that the universal will to go necessitates a particular will to take the specific path, and universal motive is not a proximate cause; particular motives are required as principles.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why is the universal will not a proximate cause of particular actions?
A) Because the universal will is weak.
B) Because particular motives (dawa'i juz'iyya) are necessary as principles.
C) Because the universal will cannot be directed.
D) Because the path is not known.

Key Takeaway
The universal motive is not a proximate cause; particular motives are necessary as principles of particular actions.

Succession of Particular Wills from the Universal Will
The author explains how universal will causes successive particular wills with a condition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a single universal will can cause a series of particular wills, with each particular will's completion being a condition for the next.

Core Idea
The universal will remains the effective cause throughout, conditioned by the completion of each particular will.

In this passage, the author delves deeper into the nature of causality concerning wills. Using the example of walking to a friend's house, he demonstrates that the overarching universal will does not vanish after the first step; rather, it persists and, under the condition that each step is completed, generates the next particular will. This explanation clarifies how a single intention can unfold into a sequence of actions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Pay attention to the walking example illustrating the causal process.
Notice that the author uses a concrete example to explain an abstract causal relationship.
2
Understand the causal relation
Identify that the universal will (al-irada al-kulliyya) is presented as the cause for each particular will (al-irada al-juz'iyya). Observe how the same universal will gives rise to the first step, then the second step, and so on.
The universal will does not disappear after the first step; it continues to act.
3
See the condition (shart)
Note the author's conclusion: the completion (inqida') of each preceding particular will is a condition for the universal will to cause the next one. Reflect on why this condition is necessary.
Without this condition, the universal will could cause all steps at once, but in reality they occur sequentially.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم ههنا بحث آخر وهو: أن تلك الإرادة الكلية تصير سبباً لحدوث إرادات متعاقبة متوالية. وذلك لأنه لما أراد الذهاب إلى ذلك الصديق، وعلم أنه لا يمكنه الوصول إلى هذا المراد، إلا برفع قدم، ووضع أخرى. فحينئذ تصير تلك الإرادة علة الحصول إرادة جزئية، لرفع القدم من ذلك الموضع الذي هو فيه، ووضعه في مكان آخر أقرب إلى دار ذلك الصديق، ثم يحدث من تلك الإرادة الكلية إرادة ثانية متعلقة برفع ذلك القدم من ذلك المكان، ووضعه في مكان آخر، أقرب من الأول، وعلى هذا الوجه فتلك الإرادة الكلية وتلك الداعية الكلية تصير سبباً لحدوث إرادة مقتضية لحدوث الخطوة الأولى. وبعد انقضاء تلك الخطوة تصير تلك الإرادة الكلية بعينها علة لحدوث إرادة ثانية مقتضية لحدوث الخطوة الثانية. فالحاصل: إن تلك الإرادة الكلية باقية أول تلك الحركة إلى آخرها ثم إنها تصير في أول الأمر علة لحدوث الإرادة الجزئية الأولى المتعلقة بالخطوة الأولى. وبعد انقضاء تلك الإرادة الجزئية الأولى تصير علة لحدوث الإرادة الجزئية الثانية المتعلقة بالخطوة الثانية. فالعلة المؤثرة في تلك الدواعي الجزئية هي تلك الداعية الكلية إلا أن انقضاء كل إرادة جزئية متقدمة، شرط لصيرورة تلك الداعية الكلية مؤثرة في حدوث الداعية الجزئية الثانية. والله ولي التوفيق.
Then here is another investigation: that that universal will becomes a cause for the occurrence of successive consecutive wills. That is because when he intended to go to that friend, and knew that he cannot reach this objective except by lifting a foot and placing another, then that will becomes a cause for the occurrence of a particular will, to lift the foot from that place where he is, and to place it in another place closer to the house of that friend. Then from that universal will arises a second will related to lifting that foot from that place and placing it in another place, closer than the first. And in this manner, that universal will and that universal motive become a cause for the occurrence of a will necessitating the occurrence of the first step. And after the completion of that step, that same universal will becomes a cause for the occurrence of a second will necessitating the occurrence of the second step. The conclusion: that that universal will remains from the beginning of that movement to its end. Then it becomes in the beginning a cause for the occurrence of the first particular will related to the first step. And after the completion of that first particular will, it becomes a cause for the occurrence of the second particular will related to the second step. So the effective cause in those particular motives is that universal motive, except that the completion of each preceding particular will is a condition for that universal motive to be effective in the occurrence of the second particular motive. And God is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 64

Commentary
This passage is a further investigation (bahth akhar) following the previous proof. The author explains how the universal will (al-irada al-kulliyya) gives rise to a succession of particular wills. Using the same walking example, he notes that the will to go to a friend leads to a particular will to lift the foot and place it closer. After that step, the same universal will causes a second particular will for the next step. The author concludes that the universal will persists throughout the entire motion, acting as a cause for each particular will in turn, but with the condition (shart) that the preceding particular will must be completed for the universal will to cause the next one. Thus, the universal motive (al-da'iya al-kulliyya) is the effective cause, but its influence is conditioned by the completion of each intermediate particular will.

Key Terms
الإرادة الكلية
الإرادة الجزئية
الداعية الكلية
الداعية الجزئية
متعاقبة متوالية
شرط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The universal will vanishes after the first particular will is produced.
Correction: The author emphasizes that the universal will remains from beginning to end and continues to cause subsequent particular wills, conditioned by the completion of each preceding step.

Checkpoint
What is the condition for the universal will to cause the second particular will?
A) The second will must be stronger.
B) The first particular will must be completed.
C) The friend must be closer.
D) The path must be clear.

Key Takeaway
The universal will is the persistent effective cause of a sequence of particular wills, with the completion of each particular will serving as the condition for the next.

The Philosophers' First Objection: Determinism from the Necessity of a Motive
The philosophers present their first objection: necessity of motive leads to determinism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' first objection against the Mu'tazila, which uses their own principle of motive to argue for determinism and undermines divine beneficence.

Core Idea
The requirement of a motive for every act paradoxically leads to determinism.

The philosophers (ḥukamā') respond to the Mu'tazila argument by turning it against them. They argue that if every act requires a motive, then human acts cannot be free unless God creates that motive—resulting in determinism. This objection challenges the Mu'tazila's view on divine beneficence and human agency.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the full Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the logical flow: premise, infinite regress, conclusion.
The philosophers structure their objection as a conditional argument.
2
Identify the structure of the objection
The passage begins with 'first' (الأول) then presents two possibilities for the motive. Trace each branch.
3
Understand the logical chain
Work through the argument step by step: act requires motive → if motive from human, infinite regress; if from God, act necessary → human acts are effects of God's acts.
Focus on how the conclusion follows from the premise.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider how this objection undermines the Mu'tazila's claim that God acts from a motive of beneficence. Why does determinism make this problematic?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالت الحكماء : هذا الكلام منقوض من وجوه : الأول : إنا قد دللنا على أن صدور الفعل عن القادر بدون الداعي محال، وإذا ثبت هذا لزم القطع بأن العبد لا يصدر عنه الفعل إلا مع الداعي، وتلك الداعية إن كانت من فعل العبد لزم التسلسل، وإن انتهت إلى داعية يخلقها الله تعالى في العبد. فقد بينا: أن عند حصول تلك الداعية يجب الفعل وعند عدم حصولها يمتنع الفعل. فيلزم القطع بأن أفعال العبد معلولات أفعال الله تعالى. وإذا كان الأمر كذلك فخالق العلة يكون خالقاً للمعلول ومريداً له. ومتى كان الأمر كذلك امتنع أن يقال: إن الشرور غير واقعة بمشيئة الله وبقضائه. وإذا كان الأمر كذلك فحينئذ يبطل قول من يقول: إن صدور الفعل عن الله موقوف على حصول داعية الإحسان.
The philosophers (ḥukamā') said: This discourse is refuted in several respects. First: We have demonstrated that the occurrence of an act from the powerful agent (al-qādir) without a motive (dā'ī) is impossible. If this is established, then it necessarily follows that the servant (al-ʿabd) does not perform an act except with a motive. If that motive is an act of the servant, an infinite regress (tasalsul) follows. And if it terminates at a motive that God creates in the servant, we have shown that when that motive occurs, the act is necessary, and when it does not occur, the act is impossible. Therefore, it necessarily follows that the acts of the servant are effects (maʿlūlāt) of the acts of God. And if this is the case, then the creator of the cause (al-ʿilla) is the creator of the effect (al-maʿlūl) and the willer (murīd) of it. When this is the case, it is impossible to say that evils do not occur by God's will and decree. And if this is the case, then the statement of whoever says that the occurrence of the act from God is dependent upon the occurrence of the motive of beneficence (dā'iyat al-iḥsān) is invalidated.
Source pages p. 72

Commentary
This passage presents the first objection of the philosophers (ḥukamā') against the Mu'tazila argument. They argue that the Mu'tazila's own principle that every act requires a motive leads to determinism. If human acts require a motive, and that motive cannot be from the human (to avoid infinite regress), then it must be created by God. Then human acts become necessary effects of God's creation, meaning all actions, including evils, are by God's will. This undermines the Mu'tazila's claim that God's acts are motivated by beneficence, because if human actions are determined, then the distinction between good and evil motives becomes problematic. The objection is a reductio ad absurdum against the Mu'tazila position.

Key Terms
القادر (al-qādir)
الداعي (al-dā'ī)
التسلسل (al-tasalsul)
المعلول (al-maʿlūl)
العلة (al-ʿilla)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The necessity of a motive applies only to God's actions, not to human actions.
Correction: The philosophers apply the principle uniformly to all agents (powerful agents), including humans. They argue that if every act requires a motive, then humans are no exception, leading to determinism.

Checkpoint
According to the philosophers' objection, if human acts require a motive created by God, what follows?
A) Humans have free will
B) Evils occur without God's will
C) God's motive of beneficence is established
D) The acts of the servant are effects of God's acts

Key Takeaway
The philosophers' objection shows that the Mu'tazila's principle of motive, if consistently applied, leads to determinism and undermines their own views on divine beneficence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Does this realization make you fear that your faith is merely a result of God's creation of a motive in you, rather than your own choice? The Philosophers' First Objection: Determinism from Causation
The philosophers argue that the proof for divine beneficence leads to determinism and undermines free will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' objection that the principle of necessary causation leads to determinism and undermines the Ash'ari position on divine beneficence.

Core Idea
If every act needs a cause, then God becomes the cause of all human acts, including evil.

The philosophers present a powerful objection to the Ash'ari proof of divine beneficence. They argue that the very premise—that an act requires a motive—leads to determinism. This objection forces us to rethink the relationship between divine causation and human responsibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the logical structure: principle, application, dilemma, conclusion.
The objection uses the agreed principle that an act requires a motive.
2
Identify the dilemma
Find the two options for the source of the motive: either the human creates it (leading to infinite regress) or God creates it (leading to necessitation).
The philosophers choose the second option to avoid regress.
3
Trace the conclusion
Follow the reasoning: if God creates the motive, the act is necessitated, so God becomes creator of both cause and effect, making all acts by His will.
This invalidates the claim that evils are not by God's decree.
4
Reflect on the impact
Consider how this objection challenges the idea that God's act is contingent on the motive of beneficence.
The objection shows a tension between divine causation and the Ash'ari position on free will.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالت الحكماء : هذا الكلام منقوض من وجوه : الأول : إنا قد دللنا على أن صدور الفعل عن القادر بدون الداعي محال ، وإذا ثبت هذا لزم القطع بأن العبد لا يصدر عنه الفعل إلا مع الداعي، وتلك الداعية إن كانت من فعل العبد لزم التسلسل . وإن انتهت إلى داعية يخلقها الله تعالى في العبد. فقد بينا : أن عند حصول تلك الداعية يجب الفعل وعند عدم حصولها يمتنع الفعل. فيلزم القطع بأن أفعال العبد معلولات أفعال الله تعالى . وإذا كان الأمر كذلك فخالق العلة ، يكون خالقاً للمعلول ، ومريداً له . ومتى كان الأمر كذلك امتنع أن يقال : إن الشرور غير واقعة بمشيئة الله وبقضائه. وإذا كان الأمر كذلك فحينئذ يبطل قول من يقول : إن صدور الفعل عن الله موقوف على حصول داعية الإحسان
The philosophers said: This speech is refuted from several aspects: First: We have demonstrated that the occurrence of an act from the capable agent without a motive is impossible. If this is established, then it necessarily follows that the servant does not perform an act except with a motive. And that motive, if it is from the servant's own act, an infinite regress follows. And if it ends at a motive that God creates in the servant, then we have shown that when that motive occurs the act is necessitated, and when it is absent the act is impossible. Hence it necessarily follows that the acts of the servant are effects of the acts of God. And if this is the case, then the Creator of the cause is the Creator of the effect and the willer of it. And when this is the case, it is impossible to say that evils do not occur by God's will and decree. And if this is the case, then the statement of those who say that the occurrence of the act from God is contingent upon the attainment of the motive of beneficence is invalidated.
Source pages p. 72

Commentary
This passage presents the first objection from the philosophers. They argue that the very principle used in the previous proof – that no act occurs without a motive – leads to consequences that contradict the Ash'ari position on free will and divine beneficence. The objection is structured as follows: First, it restates the agreed principle that an act from a capable agent requires a motive. Applied to human actions, this means that every human act is caused by a motive. That motive must either be created by the human (leading to infinite regress) or created by God. The philosophers insist it must be created by God to avoid regress. Then, given that God creates the motive, the human act necessarily follows. Therefore, God is the creator of both the cause (the motive) and the effect (the human act), and thus He wills it. This makes God the author of all human acts, including evil. Consequently, the statement that God's act (creation) is contingent on the motive of beneficence becomes untenable because it implies God's action is conditioned by something external, which the philosophers reject.

Key Terms
داعية
قادر
تسلسل
معلول
مشيئة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The philosophers' objection is an argument for free will.
Correction: Actually, the objection argues that the Ash'ari proof leads to determinism, not that humans are free. The philosophers are critiquing the proof by showing its implications.

Checkpoint
According to the first objection, what follows if the motive for a human act is created by God?
A) The human act is free.
B) The human act is necessitated, making God the creator of the act.
C) Infinite regress is avoided, but the act becomes impossible.
D) The human is still responsible for the act.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers' objection shows that applying the principle of necessary causation to human acts leads to theological determinism, challenging the Ash'ari position on free will and divine beneficence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you examine your own sinful choices, do you tend to blame your environment, upbringing, or circumstances as 'motives' that forced you? How does the philosophers' objection—that such motives ultimately trace back to God—unsettle the way you view your own responsibility before Him? Will you today repent of any sin you have justified by its 'causes'?
?
If you truly believed that God creates every motive in your heart, how would that change the intensity of your dua (supplication) when you struggle with a desire to disobey? Are you willing to ask God to change your motives, knowing that He alone controls them?

Think of a specific good deed you performed recently. Did you feel a sense of pride as if it originated from you? The philosophers' argument, though problematic, forces us to confront that even our good motives come ultimately from God. Will you thank Him for that specific act today, acknowledging that the motive itself was a gift?

The Definitions of al-Qadir and al-Mujib
Defines two types of effective agents: Qadir and Mujib.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between al-Qadir (the powerful agent who can choose) and al-Mujib (the necessitated agent) as defined by the people of religions and sects.

Core Idea
An effective agent is either powerful (able to act or not) or necessitated (must act).

Before engaging with the philosophers' critique, the author first clarifies the two types of effective agents recognized by the theologians: the powerful agent (al-Qadir) who can act or refrain based on motivations, and the necessitated agent (al-Mujib) who acts necessarily. This foundational distinction sets the stage for the subsequent philosophical debate about contingent agency.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own definitions come through before you interpret.
Notice the concise style: the author states the division and then defines each term.
2
Identify the two categories
The passage presents two types of effective agents: al-Qadir and al-Mujib. Find the defining characteristic of each.
For al-Qadir, the key phrase is 'مع جواز أن لا يؤثر' (with the possibility of not affecting). For al-Mujib, it is 'لا مع جواز أن لا يؤثر' (without the possibility of not affecting).
3
Reflect on the definition of al-Qadir
Note that al-Qadir is defined as one who can act or not act 'بحسب الدواعي' (according to motivations). This suggests that choice is based on motives, not arbitrary.
This definition will be important when the philosophers argue that complete conditions necessitate the effect.

قال أهل الملل والنحل : المؤثر إما أن يقع مع جواز أن لا يؤثر وهو القادر ، أو يؤثر لا مع جواز أن لا يؤثر ، وهو الموجب فهذا التقسيم يدل على أن كل مؤثر ، فهو إما قادر ، وإما موجب ثم عند هذا قالوا : القادر The people of religions and sects said: The effective agent is either that which occurs with the possibility of not affecting—and that is al-Qadir (the Powerful), or it affects without the possibility of not affecting—and that is al-Mujib (the Necessary Agent). So this division indicates that every effective agent is either a Qadir or a Mujib. Then upon this they said: Al-Qadir is the one from whom it is valid to affect sometimes and not to affect at other times, according to motivations. This is the summary of the discourse on the difference between al-Qadir and al-Mujib. The philosophers said: The assertion of an effective agent whose effect is on the way of possibility (sihha) and not on the way of necessity (wujub) is a problematic statement. Its explanation is from several aspects: The first proof: that everything we suppose to be effective in an effect: either everything indispensable for it to be effective in that effect is present, or the totality of those things is not present. If everything indispensable is present, then the effect must follow from it, because if it were not necessary, then it would be valid for the effect to be absent from it, and everything that is possible does not entail impossibility from assuming its occurrence. So let us suppose that totality sometimes with the occurrence of the effect, and sometimes without its occurrence. Then the specification of one of the two times for the occurrence of the effect rather than the second time is either dependent on the addition of a specificator (mukhassis) to it, or not dependent. If dependent, then this extra condition is one of the things considered in its being effective in that effect, but we had assumed that the totality obtained before this condition was all the things considered in its being effective. This is a contradictory inconsistency. And if that specification is not dependent on the addition of a condition to it, then the preponderance of a possible equal without a preponderator follows, which is impossible. And know that our saying: 'everything indispensable for effectiveness' includes everything needed for that effectiveness, such as the appropriate time, the determined benefit, the absence of obstacles, and the presence of the instrument. In sum, it includes everything indispensable, whether existential or non-existential, and whether it is an attribute of His or an existent. As for the demonstration that when everything indispensable for effectiveness is obtained, the act must occur; and as for the demonstration that when a condition among the conditions considered in effectiveness is missing, then the act is impossible: the proof for it is that upon the loss of a condition among the conditions considered in that effectiveness, if the occurrence of that effect is possible, then the occurrence of that effect would be independent of that condition, so that condition would not be considered in the effectiveness—but we have assumed that condition to be considered in the effectiveness. This is a contradiction. And if it is not possible for it to occur, then that is what is sought. If you understand this, then we say: Every effective agent in an effect: either the totality of the things considered in its being effective in that effect is obtained, or that totality is not obtained. If that totality is obtained, we have demonstrated that the effect must issue from it; and if that totality is not obtained, we have demonstrated that the act and effect are impossible to issue from it. And since there is no state except one of these two states, and it is established that what is obtained in ...
Source pages p. 78

Commentary
This passage first defines the two types of effective agents according to the people of religions and sects: al-Qadir (the Powerful), who can act or refrain from acting based on motivations, and al-Mujib (the Necessary Agent), who must act without possibility of not acting. Then it presents the philosophers' objection to the existence of an agent that acts contingently (on the way of possibility). The philosophers claim that if an agent's effect is not necessary, then the agent's effectiveness is problematic. They offer a first proof: consider any agent and its effect. If all necessary conditions for the effect are present, the effect must occur—otherwise, it would be possible for the effect to be absent, leading to a contradiction. They elaborate by supposing that the set of conditions is sometimes accompanied by the effect and sometimes not. The difference between the two cases requires a specificator (mukhassis), but if the set was already complete, adding a specificator would mean the set was not actually complete, a contradiction. If no specificator is needed, then the effect's occurrence is arbitrary, which would be a preponderance without a preponderator, also impossible. The passage then clarifies that 'everything indispensable' includes all existential and non-existential conditions, like time, benefit, absence of obstacles, and instruments. It further argues that if a condition is missing and the effect could still occur, then that condition is not truly indispensable—a contradiction. Thus, the only possibilities are that either all conditions are present (so the effect must occur) or not (so the effect cannot occur). The argument establishes that for any agent, the effect is either necessary or impossible, leaving no room for contingent action. This challenges the theological notion of a free agent who can choose to act or not.

موجب
مؤثر
دواعي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that al-Qadir's action is completely arbitrary, without any basis.
Correction: The passage clarifies that al-Qadir acts 'بحسب الدواعي' (according to motivations), indicating that the choice is based on reasons, not randomness.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what distinguishes al-Qadir from al-Mujib?
A) Al-Qadir always acts, al-Mujib may refrain.
B) Al-Qadir can act or not act; al-Mujib must act.
C) Al-Qadir is God, al-Mujib is a philosopher.
D) Al-Qadir acts without motivations, al-Mujib acts with motivations.

Key Takeaway
The theologians define two types of effective agents: al-Qadir, who can choose to act or not based on motivations, and al-Mujib, who must act necessarily.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that God could have chosen not to create you? If not, what does that say about your understanding of His power as al-Qadir?
?
When you face trials, do you assume God's action is necessary and fixed, or do you see room for His free choice based on wisdom? How does that change your prayer?
?
If God were an agent like a fire (necessitated), how would your relationship with Him differ? Does the thought of a free God make you more or less accountable?

Causality and Created Things

The Philosophers' First Proof Against Contingent Agency
Presents the first philosophical proof against contingent agency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' first logical argument that challenges the coherence of a contingent agent who could act or not act.

Core Idea
If all conditions are present, the effect must occur; if any condition is missing, the effect cannot occur.

After defining the two types of agents, the author presents the philosophers' objection to the existence of a contingent agent (al-Qadir). The philosophers argue that the notion of an agent who can act or not act when all conditions are fulfilled is logically problematic. Their first proof is a meticulous reductio ad absurdum showing that if all necessary conditions are present, the effect must follow necessarily, leaving no room for genuine contingency.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening claim
The philosophers begin by stating that the assertion of a contingent agent is problematic. Read the first few lines to grasp their starting point.
They use 'قول مشكل' (a problematic statement) to indicate they will show inconsistency.
2
Follow the first proof's logic
The proof considers an agent and its effect. It posits two cases: either all necessary conditions are present or not. Trace the reasoning that if all conditions are present, the effect must occur, and if not, it cannot occur.
Pay attention to the concepts of 'مجموع الأمور المعتبرة' (totality of considered things) and 'مخصص' (specificator).
3
Understand the 'specificator' objection
The proof argues that if we imagine the set of conditions complete but the effect sometimes occurs and sometimes not, then the difference must come from an extra specificator. But then the original set was not truly complete, leading to contradiction.
Alternatively, if no specificator is needed, then the effect's occurrence is arbitrary, which is impossible—this is a key point.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The argument concludes that for any agent, the effect is either necessary (if all conditions present) or impossible (if any condition missing). Thus, there is no room for contingent agency.
This sets up the theological response, which is not included here.

The Necessity of a Determinant for Time Specificity
Dilemma argument for time specificity determinant

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical argument that an effect cannot occur at a specific time without a determinant (mukhassis or murajjih).

Core Idea
Without a determinant, an effect's specific time is rationally impossible.

This passage presents a concise logical dilemma to prove that every effect must have a cause specifying its time of occurrence. The argument shows that assuming otherwise leads to an unavoidable contradiction or an impossible preference. Let's follow the reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow, then read the translation to understand the dilemma.
Notice the two alternatives introduced by 'إما' (either) and 'أو' (or).
2
Identify the two horns of the dilemma
The passage presents two possibilities: either the time specification depends on an additional condition or it does not. Write down each possibility in your own words.
3
Analyze the first horn (with condition)
If an additional condition is needed, then that condition was part of the cause all along, contradicting the initial assumption that the cause was complete without it.
This is a 'reductio ad absurdum' (khalf).
4
Analyze the second horn (without condition)
If no additional condition is needed, then the effect's time is preferred without any reason, which is rationally impossible (muhal).
This is the principle of sufficient reason.
5
Conclude the necessity of a determinant
Both horns lead to impossibility unless a determinant exists. Therefore, the effect must have a determinant for its specific time.
This establishes a key causal principle in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فاختصاص أحد الوقتين بحصول الأثر دون الوقت الثاني إما أن يتوقف على انضمام مخصص إليه ، أو لا يتوقف . فإن توقف فحينئذ هذا القيد الزائد أحد الأمور المعتبرة في كونه مؤثراً في ذلك الأثر ، لكنا كنا قد فرضنا أن المجموع الحاصل قبل هذا القيد ، كان كل الأمور المعتبرة في كونه مؤثراً . وهذا خلف متناقض وإما أن لم يتوقف ذلك الامتياز على انضمام قيد إليه ، فحينئذ يلزم ترجيح الممكن المتساوي من غير مرجح وهو محال
The specificity of one of the two times for the occurrence of the effect to the exclusion of the other time either depends on the addition of a specifier (mukhassis) to it, or does not depend. If it depends, then this additional condition is one of the matters considered in its being effective in that effect, but we had assumed that the total obtained before this condition was all the matters considered in its being effective. This is a contradictory consequence. And if that distinction does not depend on the addition of a condition to it, then it necessitates the preference of an equally possible thing without a determinant (murajjih), which is impossible.
Source pages p. 79

Commentary
This passage presents a logical argument about why an effect occurs at one specific time rather than another. The argument is structured as a dilemma: either the specification of a particular time depends on an additional determinant (mukhassis) or it does not. If it depends, then the initial assumption that all necessary conditions for causation were already present is contradicted, because the additional condition becomes one of the necessary conditions, reversing the original assumption. If it does not depend on a determinant, then we have a preference without a cause (tarjih bila murajjih), which is rationally impossible. Thus, the conclusion is that an effect must have a determinant for its specific time of occurrence.

Key Terms
مخصص (mukhassis)
مرجح (murajjih)
ترجيح (tarjih)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the time of an effect can be arbitrary without a cause, as long as the cause itself exists.
Correction: This argument shows that even if the cause exists, the specific time of the effect requires a separate determinant, otherwise it is a preference without a reason.

Checkpoint
What does the argument conclude about the specification of a time for an effect?
A) It always depends on an additional condition.
B) It either depends on an additional condition or leads to a contradiction.
C) It does not require any determinant.
D) It is impossible to determine.

Key Takeaway
The specific time of an effect cannot be left without a determinant; otherwise, reason demands a cause or else impossibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your daily life, you often assume that things happen at certain times for no apparent reason. After studying this lesson, how does the necessity of a determinant for time specificity challenge your casual acceptance of 'just because'? Will you now actively look for the wisdom behind timing in your own experiences?
?
This argument proves that without a determinant, reason itself would be violated. When you delay or rush actions, do you consider that every moment of your life is assigned by a wise Creator? How can you cultivate a deeper sense of trust in God's timing when your plans don't go as expected?
?
The dilemma forces you to admit that either you accept a contradiction or you admit a necessary determinant. How does this logical necessity impact your faith in God's absolute control over time? Are you willing to realign your life’s schedule with His will, even when it feels uncomfortable?

The Necessity of Conditions for Causation
Defines necessary conditions and proves necessity/impossibility of effect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational proof that the presence of all necessary conditions necessitates the effect, while the absence of any condition makes it impossible, and why permissibility is rationally unacceptable.

Core Idea
All necessary conditions for causation must be present—otherwise the effect is impossible, not merely permissible.

This passage unpacks the meaning of 'every necessary condition' in causation and gives a rigorous proof that when all conditions are met, the effect must occur; when any is missing, it is impossible. The proof uses a contradiction argument to show that mere permissibility is not a rational option. Understanding this argument is key to grasping the determinative nature of causality in kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice the examples given for necessary conditions.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Understand the definition of 'all necessary conditions'
The passage explains that 'every necessary condition' includes all requirements—whether positive or negative, attributes or external entities. Identify the examples.
This sets the stage for the proof.
3
Follow the proof for presence and absence
The proof has two parts: when all conditions are present, the effect is necessary; when any condition is missing, the effect is impossible. Follow the contradiction argument for the second part.
The proof relies on assuming a condition is necessary and then testing if the effect can occur without it.
4
Grasp the conclusion about permissibility
The passage concludes that the only two states are necessity and impossibility; any claim of 'validity' or 'permissibility' is rationally unacceptable.
This is a key point in the lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن قولنا : كل ما لا بد منه في المؤثرية : دخل فيه كل ما يحتاج إليه في تلك المؤثرية. مثل الوقت الموافق ومثل المصلحة المعينة ، ومثل انتفاء العوائق ، ومثل حصول الآلة . وبالجملة : فقد دخل فيه كل ما لا بد منه سواء كان وجودياً أو عدمياً، وسواء كان صفة من صفاته ، أو كان موجوداً . أما بيان أنه إذا حصل كل ما لا بد منه في المؤثرية ، فإنه يجب حصول الفعل، وأما بيان أنه إذا فقد قيد من القيود المعتبرة في المؤثرية ، فإنه يمتنع حصول الفعل : فالدليل عليه : أن عند فقدان قيد من القيود المعتبرة في تلك المؤثرية ، إن أمكن حصول ذلك الأثر ، فحينئذ يكون حصول ذلك الأثر غنياً عن ذلك القيد فلا يكون ذلك القيد معتبراً في المؤثرية . لكنا قد فرضنا أن ذلك القيد معتبر في المؤثرية . هذا خلف . وإن لم يكن ممكن الحصول، فذلك هو المطلوب . إذا عرفت هذا فنقول : كل مؤثر في أثر. فإما أن يكون مجموع الأمور المعتبرة في كونه مؤثراً في ذلك الأثر حاصلاً ، وإما أن لا يكون ذلك المجموع حاصلاً . فإن كان ذلك المجموع حاصلاً فقد دللنا على أنه يجب صدور ذلك الأثر عنه ، وإن كان ذلك المجموع غير حاصل، فقد دللنا على أنه يمتنع صدور الفعل والأثر عنه ، وإذا كان لا حال إلا إحدى هاتين الحالتين ، وثبت أن الحاصل في إحدى الحالتين هو الوجوب والحاصل في الحالة الثانية هو الامتناع، ظهر حينئذ أن التأثير على سبيل الصحة والجواز قول لا يقبله العقل البتة . هذا تمام الكلام في تقرير هذا السؤال
And know that our statement: 'Every necessary condition for causation' includes everything needed for that causation: such as the suitable time, the specific benefit, the absence of obstacles, and the availability of the instrument. In short, it includes everything necessary, whether existent or non-existent, and whether it is a description of His attributes or an existent. As for the demonstration that when every necessary condition for causation is present, the act must occur; and as for the demonstration that when a condition among the considered conditions for causation is missing, the act is impossible: the proof for it is that upon the absence of a condition among the considered conditions for that causation, if the occurrence of that effect is possible, then the occurrence of that effect would be independent of that condition, so that condition would not be considered in the causation. But we have assumed that that condition is considered in the causation. This is a contradiction. And if it is not possible, then that is the required conclusion. Once you understand this, we say: Every cause in relation to an effect either has the totality of the matters considered in its being a cause for that effect present, or that totality is not present. If that totality is present, we have demonstrated that the effect must issue from it; and if that totality is not present, we have demonstrated that the issuance of the act and effect from it is impossible. Since there is no state except one of these two states, and it is established that what occurs in one state is necessity and what occurs in the second state is impossibility, it becomes clear that causation on the basis of validity and permissibility is a statement that the mind does not accept at all. This completes the discussion in establishing this question.
Source pages p. 79

Commentary
This passage clarifies the meaning of 'all necessary conditions' (kull ma la budda minhu) for causation, giving examples such as the suitable time, the specific benefit, absence of obstacles, and availability of the instrument. It emphasizes that these can be either existent or non-existent conditions. Then it provides a proof for two points: (1) if all necessary conditions are present, the act must occur; (2) if any condition is missing, the act is impossible. The proof uses a contradiction argument: if the effect could occur without that condition, then that condition would not be necessary, contradicting the assumption that it is necessary. Therefore, either the effect is necessary (when all conditions are present) or impossible (when a condition is missing). Consequently, causation in terms of mere permissibility (sihha wa jawaz) is rationally unacceptable. The passage concludes that this is the full statement of the question.

Key Terms
مؤثر (mu'aththir)
أثر (athar)
قيود (quyud)
وجوب (wujub)
امتناع (imtina')
صحة (sihha)
جواز (jawaz)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that an effect can occur by mere permission even when all necessary conditions are present, i.e., the effect is neither necessary nor impossible but merely possible.
Correction: The proof shows that if all conditions are present, the effect must occur (necessity); if any condition is missing, it is impossible. There is no third rational state of 'validity' or 'permissibility' in causation. The mind rejects it.

Checkpoint
What does the proof conclude about an effect when a necessary condition is missing?
A) It may still occur but is unlikely.
B) It is impossible.
C) It depends on the power of the agent.
D) It becomes permissible.

Key Takeaway
The presence of all necessary conditions necessitates the effect; the absence of any condition makes it impossible; permissibility is rationally invalid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see a good outcome in your life, do you usually check whether all necessary conditions were present, or do you assume it was simply Allah's permission? How does this lesson change how you examine your own actions and their prerequisites?
?
Think of a time you expected something to happen but it didn’t. Could it be that you overlooked a necessary condition from the ones mentioned (time, benefit, absence of obstacles, means)? How can you apply this understanding to strengthen your reliance on Allah while still taking full responsibility for the means?
?
The passage declares that the idea of causation by mere permission is 'a statement the mind does not accept at all.' Do you ever allow yourself to think that good deeds will automatically produce good results without securing all their conditions (like sincerity, correct intention, adherence to Sunnah)? What condition are you most likely to neglect, and how will you ensure you do not neglect it from today?

Two Views of the Mutakallimun on the Determinant
Presents two sayings of the mutakallimun on the determinant.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two opposing positions within kalam regarding whether a capable agent requires a determinant for action.

Core Idea
The mutakallimun debated whether preference without a determinant is possible or impossible.

In this passage, the author presents two major responses from the mutakallimun to the objection that an effect must have a determinant. The first group accepts the necessity of a determinant but denies that it necessitates the act. The second group claims that preference without any determinant is possible for a capable agent, citing examples of free choice between identical options. Let's examine each view carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage identifying the two groups
Read the Arabic passage once, looking for the two distinct sayings. Notice how each is introduced.
2
Analyze the first group's claim
The first group concedes that preference without a determinant is impossible, but they argue that the determinant only makes the act more likely (awlawiyya), not necessary. Understand this nuance.
They want to avoid the conclusion that the agent is a necessitating cause.
3
Analyze the second group's claim and examples
The second group denies that preference without a determinant is impossible. They give examples like choosing between two identical cups. Understand their argument: if a determinant were necessary, the agent would be equivalent to a necessitating cause, which is absurd.
The examples are meant to show that choice can occur without a cause.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن المتكلمين قولهم في هذا المقام قولان : منهم من سلم أن الرجحان بدون المرجح محال . إلا أنهم زعموا : أن عند حصول المرجح يصير الفعل أولى بالوقوع ، إلا أن تلك الأولوية ، لا تنتهي إلى حد الوجوب . ومنهم من قال : إن الرجحان بدون المرجح في حق القادر غير ممتنع ، وضربوا لذلك أمثلة : منها المخير بين شرب القدحين ، ومنها المخير بين أكل الرغيفين ، ومنها الهارب من السبع إذا وصل إلى مشعب الطريقين ، فإنه يختار أحدهما دون الآخر ، لا لمرجح . قالوا : ولا بد هنا من الاعتراف بإمكان أن يصدر عن القادر أحد مقدوريه، دون الآخر لا لمرجح ، إذ لو وقفنا به على انضمام المرجح إليه وقد ثبت أن عند انضمام المرجح إليه يصير واجب الوقوع فحينئذ يلزم أن لا يبقى فرق بين الموجب وبين القادر ، لكن العلم بهذا الفرق ضروري ، فوجب الاعتراف بأن القادر يمكنه ترجيح أحد مقدوريه على الآخر من غير مرجح . واعلم أن هذين القولين لا مزيد عليهما في الجواب عن ذلك السؤال . لأنه إن ثبت أن القادر لا يصدر عنه الأثر المعين إلا عند انضمام المخصص إليه . وثبت أن عند انضمام المخصص إليه يجب الفعل ، فحينئذ يتم السؤال ، ولا يبقى عنه جواب البتة، فيثبت أنه لا يمكن دفعه إلا بواسطة النزاع في إحدى المقدمتين ، إما أن يقال إن صدور الفعل من القادر لا يتوقف على انضمام المخصص إليه ، وهو قول من يقول : صدور الفعل عن القادر لا يتوقف على انضمام الداعي إليه. وإما أن يقال : إنه وإن توقف
And know that the mutakallimun have two sayings in this place: some of them conceded that preference without a determinant is impossible, except that they claimed that when the determinant occurs, the act becomes more likely to occur, but that likelihood does not reach the level of necessity. And others said that preference without a determinant on the part of the capable agent (al-qadir) is not impossible, and they gave examples for this: among them is one who is given a choice between drinking two cups, and among them is one who is given a choice between eating two loaves, and among them is one fleeing from a predator when he reaches a fork in two roads, for he chooses one of them without a determinant. They said: There is no escape here from acknowledging the possibility that from the capable agent one of his two potential actions may issue without a determinant. For if we make it dependent on the addition of a determinant, and it is established that when the determinant is added it becomes necessary of occurrence, then it would follow that no difference remains between the necessitating cause (al-mujib) and the capable agent. But knowledge of this difference is necessary, so it is necessary to acknowledge that the capable agent can prefer one of his two potential actions over the other without a determinant. And know that these two sayings are not sufficient as an answer to that question. Because if it is established that the capable agent does not issue the specific effect except upon the addition of the specifier (al-mukhassis), and it is established that upon the addition of the specifier the act becomes necessary, then the question is complete and there remains no answer at all. Thus it is established that it cannot be repelled except by disputing one of the two premises: either by saying that the issuance of the act from the capable agent does not depend on the addition of the specifier—and this is the saying of those who say that the issuance of the act from the capable agent does not depend on the addition of the motive (al-da‘i)—or by saying that even if it does depend...
Source pages p. 80

Commentary
This passage presents two responses from the mutakallimun to the objection that an effect requires a determinant. The first group accepts that preference without a determinant is impossible, but argues that when the determinant is present, the act becomes only more likely (awlawiyya) but not necessary, thus avoiding the conclusion that the act is necessitated. The second group denies that preference without a determinant is impossible for a capable agent, giving examples of choice between two identical options (e.g., two cups, two loaves, a fork in the road) to show that the agent can choose without a cause. They argue that if the determinant were necessary, the agent would be equivalent to a necessitating cause, contradicting the obvious distinction. The author then refutes both views, stating that they do not adequately answer the question. If it is granted that the agent only acts when the specifier is present, and that with the specifier the act becomes necessary, then the objection stands. The only way to refute the objection is to deny one of the two premises: either that the agent's act depends on a specifier, or that with the specifier the act becomes necessary. The text ends by introducing the first possibility of denial.

Key Terms
مرجح (murajjih)
قادر (qadir)
مقدور (maqdur)
موجب (mujib)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that choice between identical options proves that no determinant is needed at all.
Correction: The author presents this as a claim of the second group, but he will later argue that neither view adequately answers the objection.

Checkpoint
According to the first saying, what is the effect of the determinant on the act?
A) It makes the act necessary.
B) It makes the act more likely, but not necessary.
C) It has no effect.
D) It makes the act impossible.

Key Takeaway
The mutakallimun are divided on whether a determinant is required for an agent's action, with some arguing it only increases likelihood and others arguing it is unnecessary.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you make a choice between two seemingly equal options, do you ever feel that your decision is truly free, or do you sense an unseen force tipping the scales? What does your answer reveal about your trust in your own will—and in God's will?
?
If God's actions required a determinant, would He still be worthy of worship as the All-Powerful? How does your view of His absolute freedom affect your gratitude and reliance on Him?
?
Consider a moment when you chose obedience over disobedience despite no external compulsion. Did you credit yourself for that choice, or did you see God's guidance in it? What would change if you acknowledged that even that preference could occur without a determinant—and that God alone is the ultimate source?

Refutation of the Two Views and the Way Out
Refutes both sayings and introduces necessary denials.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author finds both sayings inadequate and what premises must be denied to refute the objection.

Core Idea
Neither view resolves the problem; the only escape is to deny a key premise.

After presenting the two sayings, the author delivers a sharp critique. He argues that if we accept that the agent only acts with a specifier and that the specifier makes the act necessary, then the objection stands unchallenged. The only way out is to deny either that the act depends on a specifier or that the specifier necessitates the act. This lesson unpacks that critique.

Guided Path
1
Read the author's refutation
Read the passage from 'And know that these two sayings' to the end. Notice how the author states that neither saying suffices as an answer.
2
Understand the logical structure
The author sets up a conditional: if (a) the agent only acts with a specifier and (b) with the specifier the act becomes necessary, then the objection is complete and unanswerable. Identify these two premises.
The objection is that an effect requires a determinant.
3
Identify the two possible denials
The author says the only way to repel the objection is to dispute one of the two premises: either deny that the act depends on a specifier, or deny that with the specifier the act becomes necessary. Note that he introduces the first possibility but does not elaborate.
This sets up the next stage of the debate.
